It ended before monophthongization (4.1) and the rounding of *a (5.1), since it did not affect the rounded vowels that arose in those changes, and before the delabialization of *u(:) (5.1), which removed its conditioning.

While the attested results of <i>i̯-</i>prothesis are not uniform (see below), it is reflected in all three branches of Slavic, including peripheral zones; thus, like <i>u̯-</i>prothesis, it took place while CSl was still relatively compact. It followed the backing of *ɛ: to *a: after palatals (3.4), since anlaut *ɛ:, which was subject to prothesis, had a different outcome from the *ɛ: that followed inherited *i̯ (4). (Examples with <i>ja</i> for anlaut *ɛ: arose by later dialectal changes − the merger of *i̯ɛ: and *i̯a: in E-SSl, e.g. OCS <i>jasti</i> ‘eat’; and the retraction of *ɛ: before unpalatalized dentals in Lech, e.g. Po <i>jadł</i> |<i>jedzą</i> ‘eat-<span class="small-caps">PST</span>.<span class="small-caps">M</span>.3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>|<span class="small-caps">PL</span>’.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_57.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-057.png)

The regularity of <i>i̯-</i>prothesis has been obscured by later dialectal tendencies to eliminate *i̯<i>-</i> of either origin before <i>i</i>. The reflexes of *<i>inmɛ:n</i> (2) and *<i>(da)εi̯tεi̯</i> (3) show that the elision occurred consistently in SSl and most of ESl. The OSln Freising Fragments (later 10<sup>th</sup> c.) have <i>ge-</i> or <i>ie-</i> for /i̯ɛ/, e.g. <i>gest</i>, <i>iest</i> ‘be (<span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’; but plain <i>i-</i> rather than *<i>*gi</i> for initial <i>i-</i>, e.g. <i>iti</i> ‘go’. The OCS and OESl spellings are ambiguous, since Glagolitic and Cyrillic had no way to distinguish <i>ji</i>/<i>jь</i> from <i>i</i>; the spelling <i>krai</i>, with «и, ι» could convey /krajɪ/ ‘edge’ with the same ending as in <i>otьcь</i> ‘father’, or /kraji/ (<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>), with the same ending as in <i>otьci</i>. In OCS, the <span class="small-caps">PAP</span> of *<i>imus</i>- ‘take’ could be written as <i>imъ(š-)</i> or <b><i>j</i></b><i>emъ(š-)</i>; both of these presuppose /jɪmǝ(š)-/. Nonetheless, both OCS and OESl provide unequivocal evidence for <i>i̯-</i>prothesis before *i. In verbal roots (5), initial *i- is reflected as <i>i-</i> in unprefixed forms and after prefixes ending in vowels, i.e. environments where prothesis would have been conditioned, but as <i>ɪ (ь)</i> after prefixes ending in consonants, where prothesis would have been blocked. The cognate forms in OPo (representing Lech in general) followed a similar pattern: root-initial *i was reflected as <i>ji</i> in word-anlaut; as <i>j</i> in medial anlaut (after prefixes ending in vowels); and as zero after consonants when it was in “weak-jer” position (see 5.8). In other WSl dialects, *i underwent the same development after *i̯ in word-anlaut as elsewhere; cf. OCz <b><i>j</i></b><i>mě</i> (1) and <b><i>j</i></b><i>me</i>, <i>po<b>j</b>me</i> (5); US <b><i>j</i></b><i>mje</i> ‘take (<span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’, <i>po<b>j</b>mje</i> take (<span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_58.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-058.png)

#### 3.5.1. Prothesis before open vowels

Throughout Sl, *i̯ developed before initial *a: in lexical roots (6). On the southeastern periphery, in OCS and later Bg dialects (7), some of these roots are also attested without the glide. There was never prothesis before the conjunction *a: and its compounds (8). The adverbs *<i>a:kad</i> ‘as’ and *<i>a:mas</i> ‘to where’ show variation; the presence of the glide may have been due to contamination with the relative root *i̯- (9).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_59.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-059.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_60.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-060.png)

Outside of E-SSl, the isolated lexemes with initial *a: may have non-phonological explanations. The Sln variant <i>ágnje</i> (7) was probably influenced by La <i>agnus</i>. Uk dialectal <i>ajo</i> ‘egg’ is an innovation of children’s speech; Novg <i>aje-</i> (<i>ajesova</i> ‘egg-shover’, with ‘egg’ in the sense ‘testicle’) may be a taboo deformation. (See below for an alternative explanation.)

Because <i>i̯-</i>prothesis occurred before *a: but not *a (10), some scholars have dated it to LCSl, after quantitative distinctions had given way to qualitative ones (*a > *ɔ, *a: > *a; see 5.1); concomitantly, they have interpreted the cases of non-prothesis in E-SSl as peripheral archaisms. This late dating seems implausible for a change attested, with the same restriction to lexical morphemes, in all Sl dialects, including E-SSl in most of the eligible roots. This distribution is comparable to that of ECSl changes, not LCSl. The OCS and Bg exceptions need not be archaisms, since there was a tendency in E-SSl to elide *i̯ in other environments.

Given its restriction to lexical roots, <i>i̯-</i>prothesis before *a: was probably not sound change proper. The palatal glide cannot have been a reinterpretation of the opening phase of *a:; the transition from rest position to the full articulation of *a: would not involve the doming of the tongue characteristic of *i̯. A glottal consonant might be expected instead (and may have actually developed, though it cannot be reconstructed). On the other hand, the palatal glide could have arisen by provection rather than prothesis, from the final phase of front vowels in transition to *a: in close juncture; once reanalyzed as a distinct segment, it could have been generalized as a sandhi variant to other contexts. Presumably, there would have been a parallel tendency for *u̯ to develop in the transition from rounded vowels to *a:. This would account for certain roots in which CSl has an unexpected *u̯ before *a: and even *a, cf. Bg <i>vatral</i> ‘poker’, BCS <i>vȁtra</i>, Cz <i><sup>†</sup>vatra</i>, Uk <i>vatra</i> ‘fire’, Slk <i>vatra</i>, Po <i><sup>†</sup>watra</i>, ‘hearth’, if from PSl *a:tra: (cf. Avestan <i>āter</i> ‘fire’); OCS, OESl, <i>vonʲa</i>, OCz <i>vóně</i>, OPo <i>wonia</i> ‘smell’ <i>(*ani̯a</i>:). Note also OCz, Slk <i>vajce</i> ‘egg’ (6). Concomitantly, the loss of inherited *u̯ in PIE *<i>u̯ops</i>- > OSb, OESl, OPo <i>osa</i>, OCz <i>os</i> ‘wasp’ could have been a hypercorrection<i></i>. In this scenario, it would not be surprising to find forms without any glide at all; cf. Sln <i>ágnje</i> ‘lamb’, Novg <i>aje-</i> ‘egg’ (above).

The tendency to extend *i̯- more often than *u̯- may be linked with another change that involved *a: but not *a <i>−</i> its merger with *ɛ: after palatals (3.4). In PSl, initial *i̯a: was limited to derivatives of the pronominal root *i̯-. After the change of *i̯ɛ: to *i̯a: (3.4), the inventory grew to include other lexical roots (11). The existence of lexemes with semantically unmotivated (non-pronominal) *i̯a: may have promoted the reanalysis of sandhi off-glides as /i̯-/ and their extension as a phonotactic pattern<i></i>.

Similarly, in some LCSl dialects, there was sporadic <i>i̯-</i>prothesis before anlaut *u:₂ (PSl *au̯) (12) − not the <i>u̯-</i>prothesis that might be expected from the vowel’s articulation per se. This was probably a sandhi phenomenon, perhaps promoted by roots with *i̯u:₂ (from PSl *i̯au̯). This process did not occur consistently anywhere in Sl, and it generally did not affect the preposition/prefix *u:₂ (13). In ESl, there were hypercorrections in which inherited glides were lost before <i>u</i> (from *u:₂ and *ǫ) (14). This has been treated as a sound change, and the exceptions as Church Slavonisms, but in fact there are many lexemes in which the glide has been preserved.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_61.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-061.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_62.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-062.png)

### 3.6. Dental palatalizations

Before *i̯, dentals developed backed allophones with palatal (co)articulation: /Ti̯/ → [Tʲi̯] or [<u>T</u>i̯]. (The retraction diacritic <u>T</u> is used to cover postalveolar and dorsal articulations<i></i>.) Then the sequences were monophonemicized, and *i̯ interpreted as an offglide, which was later eliminated in a deductive change: ECSl¹ [Tʲi̯] or [<u>T</u>i̯] ⇒ /Tʲ/ or /<u>T</u>/ → ECSl² [Tʲⁱ̯ ~ Tʲ] or [<u>T</u>ⁱ̯ ~ T]. This complex of changes (“yodization,” Schenker 1995; “iotation,” Lunt 2001), may have occurred at various times for the different manners of articulation.

PSl *si̯ and *zi̯ became *ʃ and *ʒ (1), thus merging with the reflexes of 1VP *xʲ and *gʲ (see 3.2)<i></i>. PSl *li̯ and *ni̯ became either palatal *ʎ, *ɲ or palatalized dental *lʲ, *nʲ (2); *ri̯ (3) became *rʲ or post-alveolar *<u>r.</u> The reflexes will be indicated with the retraction diacritic (<u>R</u>).

The clusters *ti̯ and *di̯ (4) had diverse reflexes in LCSl (see 4.7). However, all the outcomes can be derived from a stage parallel to the sonorants, with either palatal coarticulation (*tʲ, *dʲ) or primary (alveo)palatal articulation (*c̟, *ɟ). The ECSl² reflexes will be indicated with the retraction diacritic (<u>T</u>).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_63.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-063.png)

The clusters *sti̯, *zdi̯ merged with *skʲ, *zgʲ from the 1VP (3.2). Evidently, the sibilants backed to assimilate to *<u>t</u> and *<u>d;</u> then they merged with the alveopalatals *ʃ and *ʒ; then the following stops *<u>t</u> and *<u>d</u> assimilated to hushers. (In some ESl and some SSl dialects, *<u>t</u> and *<u>d</u> became alveopalatals in all positions; see 4.7)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_64.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-064.png)

The palatalization of dentals and loss of the triggering *i̯ had a major impact on morphology. In class III verbs (*i̯e/*i̯o themes), new root allomorphs appeared throughout the present-tense system; the stem formant *i̯ was no longer in evidence (6). In Class IV verbs, the theme had appeared in the <i>e-</i>grade *εi̯ or the lengthened grade *i: before consonantal suffixes, and in the zero-grade *i̯ before vocalic suffixes. After dental palatalization, the allomorphy shifted to the root; with the loss of the *i̯ and the development of nasal vowels (see 4.2), the old theme was no longer apparent throughout the paradigm. Where the predesinential vowel persisted, it was necessarily reinterpreted as part of the ending (7).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_65.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-065.png)

#### 3.6.1. *kt clusters

As noted in 3.1.3, in *kt clusters the velar was lost, while the dental had bifurcating reflexes. It persisted before non-front vowels (1), but became t (see 3.6) before front vowels (2).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_66.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-066.png)

The development here was presumably not *kt > *[k]ti̯, as the drift in CSl was to eliminate postconsonantal *i̯ (see 3.7). Rather, *t developed a non-anterior allophone [<u>t</u>] in assimilation to the surrounding non-anterior segments; this merged with the phonetically similar or identical monophonemicized reflex of *ti̯<i></i>. When the preceding velar was elided, the conditioning of *<u>t</u> became unpredictable. (For the LCSl development of *t, see 4.7.)

There may have been a parallel change *n > *<u>n</u> between velars and *i<i></i>. Here BCS and some Sln dialects have /ɲ/: *<i>(−)gni:da</i>: > Sln <i>ugnjída</i> ‘ulcer’, BCS <i>gnjȉda</i> ‘nits’. In OCS <i>ognʲь</i> ‘fire’ (PSl *<i>agnis</i>), the nasal is often written with the palatalization diacritic (н̑); however, the word is also attested as a <i>i̯o-</i>stem. Elsewhere in Sl, there is principled uncertainty, as the reflexes of *<u>n</u> cannot be distinguished from those of *n before front vowels.

### 3.7. Elimination of glides in clusters

#### 3.7.1. Homorganic glides

After conditioning the 1VP (3.2) and dental palatalization (3.6), *i̯ was reinterpreted as an off-glide of the new palatals and ceased to exist as an independent unit (1−2): /ʃi̯/ → [ʃi̯] ⇒ /ʃ/ → [ʃi̯~ʃⁱ̯ ~ ʃ]. There was a parallel reanalysis of *u̯ after labial consonants; the given clusters occurred in zero-grade forms of ‘be’ (3) and over prefix boundaries (4): /bu̯/ → [bu̯] ⇒ /b/ → [bu̯~bᵘ̯~b]. (Counterexamples in the attested languages are the result of post-CSl changes or leveling: OESl <i>obiniti</i> but Ru <i>obvinit’</i> ‘accuse’, cf. <i>vina</i> ‘fault’.) The changes in homorganic glides are traditionally presented as elision. This is accurate as a metalinguistic description of the outcomes, but the essential innovations were monophonemicizations, or <i>monophthongizations</i> in Andersen’s sense (1972). The changes differed from previous developments in consonant clusters in two ways: they worked progressively, and they involved sequences that did not violate canonical syllable structure (see 3.1).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_67.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-067.png)

#### 3.7.2. Labial + glide clusters

CSl *i̯ became *<u>l</u> after labials (1−2). This affected not only inherited *i̯ (1−2) but also the *i̯ on-glide that developed in the monophthongization of *ɛu̯ (see 4.1). It is unclear whether the change began before or after the monophthongization. The lateral has traditionally been called <i>epenthetic <u>l</u></i>, as if it were inserted for ease of pronunciation. In fact, the *<u>l</u> was a transmutation of the *i̯, not an addition to the cluster. The phonetic mechanism was target undershoot as the tongue blade retracted from the rest position characteristic for labials to the domed position characteristic for *i̯<i></i>. The resulting not-quite-palatal approximant was perceptually similar both to *i̯ and to *<u>l</u> from *li̯ sequences (see 3.6), and hence open to rephonologization as *<u>l.</u>

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_68.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-068.png)

The *i̯ > *<u>l</u> change is usually treated alongside dental palatalizations [3.6], as if it involved palatal (co)articulation; in fact, it was a progressive rather than a regressive change. There are parallels from the historical period in which <i>i̯</i> became <i><u>l</u></i> in new Pi̯ clusters: PSl mai̯x- > LCSl¹ *<i>mæʃina</i> > BCS <i>mjèšina ~ <sup>†</sup>mljèšina</i> ‘sack’. There are also counter-parallels with ʎ being rephonologized as <i>i̯</i>: Rom <i>Mileta</i> > LCSl¹ *<i>mɪlætǝ</i> > BCS <i>Mljȅt</i> ~ <i><sup>†</sup>Mjȅt</i> (name of an Adriatic island); cf. La <i>planus</i> > Italian <i>piano</i> ‘level’; La <i>sclavus</i> > Italian <i>schiavo</i> ‘Slav; slave’.

The change of *i̯ > *<u>l</u> is attested in all Sl dialects within morphemes (1−2). Over morpheme boundaries (3−4), the lateral has been preserved consistently in ESl and W-SSl. Elsewhere, there was a late tendency to reanalyze it as a glide, presumably by leveling with related forms. This reversal was ongoing in OCS (10<sup>th</sup>−11<sup>th</sup> centuries), as seen in the spelling variants <i>Plʲ</i>~<i>Pj~Pьj</i> [Pj]. In WSl, *l was lost consistently in paradigmatic alternations (3) and often elsewhere (4). However, non-alternating *l has left many relics: OPo ‘heron’ (5), US <i>ča<b>pl</b>a</i>, LS <i>ca<b>pl</b>a</i>, Kb <i>cza<b>pl</b>a</i>; OCz toponyms <i>Počě<b>pl</b>ice</i>, <i>Počěpli</i>, <i>Poča<b>pl</b>i</i>; the doublets in OCz and OPo ‘mound’ (6), Slk <i>hro<b>bl’</b>a</i> ‘dam’, US <i>hrobla</i> ‘ditch’. This variation shows that the WSl loss of the lateral was relatively late and probably independent of the parallel tendency in E-SSl. (Alternatively, the variation could have arisen not by a “loss” of *<u>l</u> but by the coexistence of conservative and innovative forms within the same speech communities.)

The change led to morphophonemic alternations in verbs. In class IIIb, *<u>l</u> appeared throughout the <span class="small-caps">PRS</span> system: OCS <i>priimati</i>|<i>priemlʲetъ</i>, <i>priemlʲi</i>, <i>priemlʲǫšt-</i> ‘receive (<span class="small-caps">INF</span>|<span class="small-caps">PRS</span>. 3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>, <span class="small-caps">IMP</span>.2<span class="small-caps">SG</span>, <span class="small-caps">PRAP</span>)’. In class IV, *<u>l</u> appeared in the 1<span class="small-caps">SG</span>, but not in other <span class="small-caps">PRS</span> forms: OESl <i>lʲublʲu</i>|<i>lʲubiši</i> ‘love (<span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.1<span class="small-caps">SG</span>|2<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’. In class IVa, *<u>l</u> occurred in the stems of the imperfect, archaic <span class="small-caps">PAP</span>, <span class="small-caps">PPP</span>, and deverbal noun: BCS <i>ljubljaše</i> (<span class="small-caps">IMPF</span>.3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>); OESl <i>vъzljublʲь</i>(<span class="small-caps">PFV</span>.<span class="small-caps">PAP</span>); BCS <i>ljubljen</i> (<span class="small-caps">PPP</span>); Sln <i>ljubljenje</i> (noun).

### 3.8. Vowels in final closed syllables

In certain grammatical forms, PSl *a(:) and *ɛ(:) in final syllables rose to, or were replaced by, *u(:) and *i(:). The same happened before final sonorants; cf. *<i>ma:ti</i>: and *<i>dukti</i>: in 3.1.4. There is debate about whether the raised outcomes arose in sound changes limited to final syllables <i>(Auslautgesetze)</i> or in morphological substitutions.

For PSl *-aN (1−2), the expected outcome was *-a (see 3.1.4); the actual reflex was *u, fronted to *i after palatals (see 3.4). In the <i>(i̯)o-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">ACC</span> (1), the inherited ending would have merged with <span class="small-caps">N</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span> *-ad > *-a. If the ending *-u did not arise by sound change, it could have been imported from the <i>u-</i>stems (*-um); other <i>u-</i>stem endings were coopted during the CSl period. In the nonsigmatic (root) <span class="small-caps">AOR</span>.1<span class="small-caps">SG</span>, the expected reflex of *<i>-am</i> (cf. Gk <i>-on</i>) would have been *-a; the attested *u (<i>izid<b>ъ</b></i> ‘go out’) may have been reformed on the basis of the sigmatic <span class="small-caps">AOR</span>.1<span class="small-caps">SG</span> <i>*-s-um</i> (PreSl *<i>-sm̥</i>; cf. Gk <i>-sa</i>). The sigmatic aorist became the predominant type during the CSl period, and the two types otherwise interacted; for example, the root aorist ending was coopted to repair the erosion of the sigmatic 2/3<span class="small-caps">SG</span> in Leskien I, II, and V.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_69.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-069.png)

For PSl *-as, the expected outcome was *a, fronted to *ɛ after palatals. This occurs in lexemes where *-as was part of the stem (3). However, in the <i>(i̯)o-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span> (4), *<i>-a₂s</i> (with the subscript signaling a divergent outcome) rose to, or was replaced by, *<i>-u</i>, fronted to *<i>-i</i> after palatals. If *<i>-a₂s</i> had developed as expected, it would have been syncretic with the <span class="small-caps">N</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span> *<i>-a</i> < *<i>-ad</i> (not to mention the <span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>, see above). Such syncretism may account for seemingly “neuter” hypocoristics used for male referents, e.g. OSb <i>Radivoje</i>, OESl <i>Stoiko</i>, OCz <i>Tenko</i>, OPo <i>Zdięto</i>. (Conversely, the syncretism may explain why most PreSl barytone neuters became masculines.) While attempts have been made to explain the *<i>-a₂s</i> reflex by sound change, it was more probably analogical to the <i>u-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span> *<i>u</i> (< *<i>-us</i>/*<i>-um</i>). For the hypocoristics, syncretism was promoted by the fact that the neuter was already used to denote baby humans and animals (cf. *<i>agnɛnt</i> ‘lamb’ [6]). The directional adverbial suffix *<i>-mas</i> yielded both *<i>-ma</i> and *<i>mu</i> (5). The latter may be analogical to <span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span> *<i>-u</i> or <span class="small-caps">DAT</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span> *<i>-mu(s)</i>; accusative and dative both had directional meanings.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_70.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-070.png)

The sequence *-Vnt (<i>nt-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>) developed like internal *VN-C (6); the vowel and nasal monophthongized as Ṽ (see 4.2). By contrast, *<i>-</i>Vns (<i>o-</i>stem and <i>ā-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>) had different outcomes, depending on the preceding consonant. After non-palatals, the vowel was lengthened without being nasalized (7): *-ins > *-i:, *-uns > *-u:, and *ans > *-u: (with raising). If this was a sound change, the lengthening was compensatory for the disappearing nasal; vowels otherwise did not undergo lengthening before final *s. (If the final *s had disappeared before the nasal, **VN > **Ṽ would be expected; see 3.1.4) When *ans followed a palatal, the loss of the nasal was delayed, for unclear reasons. The vowel was fronted to *ɛ, as expected (3.4). In WSl and ESl, *-ɛns > *ɛ:, parallel to the development of *-ins. In SSl, the vowel underwent both lengthening and nasalization, like internal tautosyllabic *ɛN (see 4.2): OCS <i>ę</i> [<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]], OSb <i>e</i> [ɛ]. If the development was regular, the outcomes may be due to different relative chronologies: SSl *-ɛns > *-<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]:ⁿs > *-<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]:, but WSl, ESl *-ɛ:ⁿs > *-ɛ:s prior to nasalization. In (8), *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]: would have yielded OESl <i>a</i>, OPo <i>ę</i> [<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]], while *ɛ: would have given OCS <i>-ě</i> [æ], OSb <i>-ě</i> [e]. (The WSl and ESl outcome is known as *ɛ:₃, where *ɛ:₁ = PSl *ɛ: [2.1] and *ɛ:₂ = PSl *ai̯ [4.1]. However, it seems likely *ɛ:₃ developed prior to *ɛ:₂.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_71.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-071.png)

The outcome of *<i>-ants</i># (<span class="small-caps">PRAP</span>.<span class="small-caps">M</span>/<span class="small-caps">N</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>) also bifurcated, with the same dialect divide. After non-palatals, the vowel was lengthened, and the nasal was lost before the development of nasal monophthongs in MCSl¹. It was raised to *<i>-u</i>: in SSl but remained low *<i>a</i>: in WSl and ESl (6). After palatals, the loss of the nasal was again delayed; all dialects show the expected fronting, then lengthening and nasalization (7). (There is no way to determine if the vowel was also raised, as MCSl¹ *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]: and *ĩ: merged in LCSl.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_72.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-072.png)

## 4. Middle Common Slavic (MCSl) changes

If ECSl was the period in which the vowel system was essentially the same as in PSl, MCSl began with a drastic restructuring of the vowel system − the monophthongization of diphthongs (4.1−4.2) or <i>First Slavic Vowel Shift</i> (Andersen 1998), which probably occurred ca. 4<sup>th</sup>−5<sup>th</sup> centuries (idem 2014: 59). Like the ECSl changes, the monophthongizations had the same results in all the CSl dialects, but they were the preludes to developments with dialectally differentiated outcomes (4.4−4.6). The latter are treated as MCSl changes here if their isoglosses divide a unified WSl from SSl and ESl − that is, if CzSlk patterns with Lech. This distribution can be interpretated as follows: P-WSl extended into the Northern European Plain, which attenuated its contact with the other dialects, but it was still internally cohesive because all of its dialects were on the same side of the Carpathians. The P-ESl zone had been attenuated by northward migrations; thus pre-Novgorodian (Novg) patterned as a periphery to the central zone represented by P-SSl and P-ESl.

### 4.1. Monophthongizations of glide diphthongs

ECSl had falling diphthongs with coda *i̯ and *u̯ before consonants or the word boundary. These tautosyllabic diphthongs became long/tense monophthongs: (1) PSl *ai̯ > LCSl¹ *æ (Slavistic <i>ě₂</i>), merging with the reflex of inherited *ɛ: (1); PSl *εi̯ > LCSl¹ *i (Slavistic <i>i₂</i>), merging with the reflex of inherited *i: (2); and PSl *au̯, *ɛu̯ > LCSl¹ *u (Slavistic <i>u₂</i>) (3−4). Heterosyllabic sequences of the same vowels and glides did not undergo monophthongization; examples from the same roots are included in the table for comparison.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_73.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-073.png)

The split of tautosyllabic and heterosyllabic *au̯ created allomorphy in Leskien III verbs with stems in *-au̯- (3). The resulting morphophonemic alternation is still productive in the denominal suffix <i>-ova-</i>: Cz <i>googlovat</i>|<i>googluje</i>, Ru <i>guglovat’</i>|<i>guglujet</i> ‘Google (<span class="small-caps">INF</span>|<span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’.

The monophthongizations took place in several phases. First, the vowels assimilated to the tonality of the glides, if different: before *i̯, *a became advanced *a̟, with the tongue slightly domed and closer to the palate; before u̯, *a, *ɛ became slightly rounded *a̹, *ɛ̹. Second, the vowels tensed in partial assimilation to the close, peripheral articulation of the glide: *a̟i̯ > *a̟ˑi̯, *εi̯ > *εˑi̯, *a̹u̯ > *a̹ˑu̯, and *ɛ̹u̯ > *ɛ̹ˑu̯ > *ⁱ̯a̹ˑu̯. The breaking of the *ɛ̹ into a high-tonality opening and a low-tonality nucleus is a well-attested type of change. The front on-glide conditioned dental palatalization (3.6), which was not ordinarily an effect of *ɛ. Afterwards, it was reinterpreted as an off-glide of the preceding consonant, in accordance with the general CSl pattern (see 3.3, 3.6, 3.7.1). After labials, it became *l (3.7.2).

The stage of monophthongizations proper is designated MCSl¹. The tensed vowels became more moved along the peripheral track: *a̟ˑi̯ > *æˑⁱ̯ encroached on *εˑi̯, which rose to *eˑⁱ̯; *a̹ˑu̯ > *ɔˑᵘ̯. The glides were then revaluated as the closing phases of the long/tense vowels: [eˑi̯]⇒/*e:/ → [e:⁽ⁱ̯⁾]; [*æˑⁱ̯] ⇒ /ɛ:/ → [ɛ:⁽ⁱ̯⁾], merging with inherited *ɛ: (a near-open vowel); and [a̹ˑu̯]⇒ /ɔ:/ → [ɔ:⁽ᵘ̯⁾]. The dephonologized off-glides were later eliminated by deductive changes.

The final stage, designated MCSl²<i></i>, the mid- or close-mid monophthongs rose one cardinal position. Thus *e: merged with the reflex of inherited *i:. In rising, *ɔ: encroached on the space of inherited *u:₁, which moved into the central vowel space (see 4.3); *ɔ: then occupied the vacated close back space as *u:₂.

As a result of the monophthongizations, the inherited ablaut relations ceased to be transparent in syllables that had been diphthongal. Thus the ablaut series *i|*εi̯|*ai̯ became *i|*i:₂ |* ɛ:₂ (5), while *u|*ɛu̯|*au̯ became *u|*i̯u:₂ |*u:₂ (6).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_74.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-074.png)

#### 4.1.1. Chronology

As the reflex of *ai̯ did not condition the 1VP (3.2), it evidently was not yet a front vowel during that period. The reflex of *ɛu̯ was not yet a close vowel at the time of Vowel Fronting, since it had a different fate from inherited *i̯u: (see 3.3). (On the relative chronology of monophthongization and the Progressive Palatalization, see 4.6)

The monophthongizations <i>sensu stricto</i> had not begun during the main period of contacts with EGmc (ca. 2<sup>nd</sup>−4<sup>th</sup> cc.), since both borrowed and inherited *au̯ and *ai̯ underwent the same changes (5). However, the changes were at the MCSl¹ stage by the time of early contacts with WGmc and Rom (from ca. 5<sup>th</sup> c.). Foreign *ɔ: or o: (6) was adapted as *ɔ:, the reflex of *au̯, prior to its rise to *u:₂ in MCSl². Gk toponyms of Sl origin, dating at earliest from the late 6<sup>th</sup>−early 7<sup>th</sup> centuries, were accessed during the MCSl¹ stage, as the *au̯ reflex was rendered «ο, ω» rather than «ου»: PSl *<i>strau̯mɛn</i>-‘stream’ ⥬ Στρώμην; PSl *<i>sau̯x</i>- ‘dry’ ⥬ Σωχός, Σοχᾶς. Similarly, early BFi borrowings (probably 7<sup>th</sup>−8<sup>th</sup> centuries) show a MCSl¹ reflex of *au̯: PSl *<i>gau̯minad</i> ‘threshing floor’ ⥬ Votic <i>kōmina</i>, Vepsian <i>gomin</i>, Karelian <i>kuomino</i>, Eastern Finnish <i>kuomina</i> (from *ō).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_75.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-075.png)

#### 4.1.2. Alternative approaches

The monophthongizations have been interpreted in strikingly different ways. In one approach (e.g. Jakobson 1963), the vowel and glide metathesized in obedience to the Law of Open Syllables; the vowel was lengthened and raised: *εi̯ > *i̯e::> i₂, *ai̯ > *i̯ɛ:₂, *au̯ > *u̯ɔ: > *u:₂. However, the *εu̯ outcome cannot be explained by metathesis (**u̯e:), so an intensity shift is posited instead (*i̯u:₂). This approach does not account for why dentals and labials underwent iotation changes before *i̯u:₂, but not before *i̯e: and *i̯ɛ:₂; nor does it provide a coherent motivation for the lengthening and raising of the vowels.

Another approach (Feldstein 2003) assumes that the diphthongs were not VV̯ but VV (“equal vocalic components”, ibid.: 249). When the components matched in tonality, the first assimilated totally to the second: <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-076.png" alt="Inline image: 07_chapter01_fig_76.png"> in the present notation). When they differed in tonality, frontness spread from one to the other; then one assimilated totally to the other in sonority: <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-077.png" alt="Inline image: 07_chapter01_fig_77.png">*u̟ in the present notation). The notion of “total assimilation” conflicts with the evidence that there was an intermediate stage with mid-vowels (MCSl¹, 4.1.1). The author does not explain why “total assimilation” proceeded right to left for *a͡i, but left to right elsewhere; nor does he account for how his *ü(:) avoided merger with the front(ed) rounded vowel *u̟: (3.3), which was still labialized at the time of the Progressive Velar Palatalization (see 4.3, 4.6). He treats the development of *i̯ before *u̟u̟ (*üü) not as an integral outcome of the monophthongization but as a separate (and ad-hoc) change that “provided additional redundancy” for frontness combined with rounding (ibid.: 261).

#### 4.1.3. Unexpected outcomes

Final *-ai̯ has the regular outcome *ɛ:₂ in some endings, e.g. PSl *<i>zei̯mai̯</i> > OCS, OESl, OCz<i>zimě</i>, OPo <i>zimie</i> ‘winter (<span class="small-caps">DAT</span>/<span class="small-caps">LOC</span>)’. However, in four endings they are said to have yielded *-i:₂. Of these, the <span class="small-caps">DAT</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span> enclitic personal pronouns − OCS <i>mi</i> (1<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)<i>, ti</i> (2<span class="small-caps">SG</span>), <i>si</i> (<span class="small-caps">REFL</span>) − can be excluded. Though compared with Gk μοι, σοι, they are more likely to be the regular outcomes of PSl *<i>mɛi̯, *tɛi̯, *sɛi̯</i>; the same ending occurred in the <span class="small-caps">DAT</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span> of <i>i-</i> and consonant-stem nouns (OCS <i>pǫt-i</i> ‘road’, <i>dьn-i</i> ‘day’), cf. OPr <i>mennei, tebbei, sebbei</i>, Li <sup>†</sup><i>manie</i>, <sup>†</sup><i>tavie</i>, <sup>†</sup><i>savie</i>. The remaining endings almost certainly do go back to *-ai̯, since they triggered the 2VP (4.4) rather than the 1VP (3.2): <i>o-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span> *<i>u̯ilkai̯</i> > OCS <i>vlъci</i>, OESl <i>vьlci</i>, OCz <i>vlci</i>, OPo <i>wilcy</i> ‘wolves’; Leskien I−II <span class="small-caps">IMP</span>.2<span class="small-caps">SG</span>|3<span class="small-caps">SG</span>: <i>*mag-ai̯-s</i>/<i>-t</i> > OCS <i>modzi</i>, OESl, OCz <i>mozi</i> ‘dare’. Moreover, forms of the imperative where the diphthong was non-final have the expected outcome *ɛ:₂: OCS <i>modz<b>ě</b>te</i>, OESl <i>moz<b>ě</b>te</i>, OCz <i>moz<b>ě</b>te</i> (<span class="small-caps">IMP</span>.2<span class="small-caps">PL</span>). The problematic endings can be treated as analogical to inflection types with thematic *-i̯- (<i>i̯o-</i>stem nouns and Leskien III verbs). Alternatively, one could posit that *-ai̯C# regularly became *-i:₂, if the <i>o-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span> is reconstructed with a final *-s (cf. <i>i-</i>stem *<i>-ii̯ɛs, u-</i>stem *<i>-au̯ɛs</i>, consonant-stem *<i>-ɛs</i>).

### 4.2. Monophthongization of vowel-nasal diphthongs

In MCSl, tautosyllabic vowel-nasal diphthongs became unitary nasal vowels: *aN, *un > LCSl¹ *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] (1), and *ɛn, *in > LCSl¹ *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]. These are the sounds represented by the OCS “jusy” ѫ <i>(ǫ)</i> and ѧ <i>(ę)</i>, respectively. (For their outcomes in the individual Sl languages, see 5.2.2)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_78.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-078.png)

The development of nasal vowels took place in three phases. First, the vowels underwent assimilatory nasalization: /VN/ → [ṼN]/__{C, #}. Second, the nasals were reinterpreted as the closing phases of the vowels: [ṼN] → /Ṽ:/ → [Ṽ<sup>(N)</sup>]. In other words, they were revaluated as transitions to the closure (complete or partial) of the following units. Concomitantly, their distinctive tonality (*m or *n) was ascribed to the following consonants. Thus syllable-final nasals ceased to exist as phonologically independent units. The new nasal vowels were redundantly long, preserving the mora count of the old diphthongs.

The stage that resulted from these first two phases is designated MCSl¹<i></i>, corresponding to the monophthongization phase of vowel-glide diphthongs (see 4.1). In the next phase, MCSl²<i></i>, *ã: and *ũ: merged as mid-back rounded *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]: (Slavistic <i>ǫ</i>), and *ĩ: and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]: as (open-)mid-front <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]: (Slavistic <i>ę</i>). It is not problematic that the merger involved both lowering of the close vowels and raising of the (near-) open ones. Cross-linguistically, close nasal vowels tend to be perceived as more open, and open nasal vowels as more close, than their oral counterparts. Presumably, the mergers were also facilitated by the relative perceptual difficulty of distinguishing between nasal vowels, as compared with their oral counterparts (see Ohala 1975: 294). (The existence of a separate MCSl¹ stage, with distinct *ĩ: and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]: vowels, is indicated by the fact that the Progressive Velar Palatalization occurred after *in, but not after *ɛn; see 4.6)

The monophthongization created morphophonemic alternations between VN and <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-v-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: V with tilde]">[image-glyph: V with tilde] in nasal-stem verbs (Leskien Ia and IIIa) (4−5) and in neuter <i>n</i>-stem nouns (6). In addition, it obscured the ablaut patterns in inceptive verbs with infixed *n in the present system (7−8).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_79.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-079.png)

4.2.1. For Feldstein (2003), this change, like the other CSl monophthongizations, involved gemination in a diphthong defined as “equal vocalic components” (Feldstein 2003: 249) − i.e. VN̥. The first portion assimilated to the nasality of the second, which then assimilated to the sonority of the first: VN̥ > ṼN > ṼṼ (Feldstein 2003: 262−263). However, the starting nucleus VN̥ is typologically improbable, whereas the assimilation/ monophthongization process described in 4.2 is well attested cross-linguistically.

### 4.3. Delabialization of *u(:)1 and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-080.png" alt="Inline image: 07_chapter01_fig_80.png">(:)₁

As mentioned in 4.1, as *ɔ: (from PSl *au̯, *ɛu̯) rose, it impinged on the phonetic space of inherited *u:₁. As a result, *u:₁ centralized and delabialized, not necessarily in that order (1): *u: > (*ɯ >) *ɨ: (Slavistic <i>y</i>). Short *u underwent a parallel centralization (2): *u > MCSl² *ʊ or *ɵ (2); however, its reflex was still somewhat rounded in LCSl, at least in some peripheral dialects (see 5.1.1, 5.7). These developments evidently added to the push-chain: *u̟(:), which had arisen after palatals (see 3.3), moved into the front vowel space and eventually merged with *i(:) (3). This merger had not yet happened in the early phases of the Progressive Velar Palatalization (4.6), which was conditioned by *i(:) but not *u̟(:).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_81.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-081.png)

Some of the Balkan Rom (post-Vulgar La) dialects with which migrating Slavophones had contact had front rounded vowels *u̟ and *o̟. These were adapted as MCSl *u̟: (4) or, after non-palatals, *i̯u̟: (5), with the vowel diphthongized to accommodate CSl phonotactics (cf. 3.4). As shown in (5), *i̯ caused dental palatalization (see 3.6); then it was eliminated after palatals, in accordance with the usual CSl pattern (see 3.7). (Cf. Lombard <i>lačüga</i>. La <i>lactūca</i> could not have been the direct source of MCSl *<i>lac̟u̟:ka</i>:, as *kt would be expected to yield *t; nor can the donor have been ERom: Romanian <i>lăptucă</i>.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_82.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-082.png)

The delabialization of *u(:) had not yet taken place during early Migration-Period (probably 6<sup>th</sup>−7<sup>th</sup>-c.) contacts with Gk and La. Slavophone settlers in Byzantine Dalmatia adapted the *u(:) of Rom toponyms as their own *u(:)₁ (>*ɨ: > OCr <i>i</i>): <i>Tragurium</i> > MCSl¹ *<i>tragu:ri</i>- > LCSl¹ *<i>trogɨrɪ</i> > <i>Trogir; Scardona</i> > *<i>skardu:na</i> ⥬ MCSl¹ *<i>skardu:nu</i> > LCSl¹ *<i>skardɨnʊ</i> > <i>Skradin</i>. Likewise, Slavophones adapted OHG <i>u</i>: as their own MCSl¹ *u:₁ (> *ɨ:) rather than MCSl² *u:₂ (6). Conversely, Frankish sources of the 7<sup>th</sup>−8<sup>th</sup> centuries render CSl *u:₁ as <i>u</i> rather than, say, <i>i, oi</i>, or <i>ui: *u̯aldu:ka</i>: ‘ruler’ ⥬ <i>Walducus; *dabramu:slis</i> ⥬ <i>Dabramuzli</i>. Similarly, some Gk loanwords and toponyms of CSl provenience, presumably dating from the early period of settlements (late 6<sup>th</sup>− 7<sup>th</sup>-centuries) show <i>u</i> (ου) for *u(:)₁ instead of <i>ü</i> (υ) or <i>i</i> (ι, ει, η): *<i>magu:la</i>: (1) ⥬ μαγούλα ‘hill’ (also well represented in toponyms); *<i>buz-</i> ‘elderberry’ ⥬ Βούζι[ον] (toponym), cf. Bg <i>bŭz</i>, BCS <i>baz</i>, Ru <i>boz</i>, Cz, Po <i>bez</i>.

### 4.4. Second Regressive Palatalization of Velars

The Second Palatalization of Velars (2VP) was conditioned by the reflexes of PSl *ai̯ (see 4.1) − MCSl¹ *ɛ:₂ (1−2), and *i: from *ai̯C# (3−4). It was also triggered by *i(:) in Migration-Period loanwords (5). It was the first CSl sound change to have dialectally diverse outcomes: *k > <i>ts</i>, *g > <i>(d)z</i>, but *x > <i>ʃ</i> in WSl, <i>xʲ</i> in Novg, and <i>s</i> elsewhere. These isoglosses suggest that the 2VP was happening as P-WSl speakers were migrating north of the Carpathians, and as P-ESl (P-Novg) speakers moved into northwestern Russia (see 1.1.2).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_83.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-083.png)

The change proceeded in several phases. In 2VP¹, velars developed fronted allophones, probably with palatal coarticulation: *k > *kʲ, *g > *gʲ, *x > *xʲ<i></i>. This stage is attested in Novg, on the northeastern periphery. In 2VP², the palatalized velars became palatals: *kʲ > *c̟, *gʲ > *ɟ, *xʲ > *ç. At this stage, P-WSl *ç merged with alveopalatal *ʃ from 1VP *x and from *si̯ (3.2, 3.6). In 2VP³, the palatals became dentals with palatal coarticulation: *c̟ > *tʲ, *ɟ > *dʲ, and, in P-ESl and P-SSl, *ç > *sʲ. The dental stops became affricates in all dialects: *tʲ > *tsʲ, *dʲ > *dzʲ (Slavistic <i>c, dz</i> or <i>ʒ</i>, and <i>š</i>). The voiced affricate was preserved as such in E-SSl, Slk, and Lech; otherwise it was lenited to *zʲ except when there was a preceding *z (see [11] below).

For 2VP *xʲ, CenSlk has twofold reflexes − WSl <i>ʃ</i> in non-alternating environments, and quasi-SSl <i>s</i> stem-finally: <i>šerý</i> ‘grey’, but <i>blcha</i>|<i>blse</i> ‘flea (<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>|<span class="small-caps">LOC</span>)’; cf. OCz <i>blcha</i>|<i>blšě</i> ‘flea’ (PSl *<i>bluxa</i>:). The mixed outcome may be due to language contact; prior to the 10<sup>th</sup> c., CenSlk neighbored W-SSl, and it has SSl-like reflexes from certain other changes. It is also conceivable that the *s was analogical to the velar-to-dental mutations from *k and *g (2).

The 2VP reflexes of *skʲ (6−8) and the rare *zgʲ (9) show a more complicated dialect split. For SSl, WSl, and Novg, the outcomes were homorganic with the *xʲ reflexes. Thus, SSl had palatalized dental *sʲtsʲ and *zʲdzʲ; with the loss of palatal coarticulation, these are reflected as <i>st(s)</i> (<i>ts</i> in some dialects) and <i>zd</i>. WSl had alveopalatal *ʃtʃ and *ʒdʒ; these became <i>ʃc̟</i> [<i>št’</i>] and <i>ʒɟ</i> [žd’] in WCz. The alveopalatal outcomes in WSl presuppose that the sibilants first assimilated to the palatal (co)articulation of the stops: *skʲ > *sʲkʲ (or *sc̟) > *sʲc̟; *zgʲ >*zʲgʲ (or *zɟ) >*zʲɟ. This may also have occurred in the other dialects; cf. the reflex <i>ʃk</i> that appears in a few lexemes in ESl (8), as well as in CzSlk (OCz <i>škieřiti</i>~<i>ščeřiti</i>), if it is not affective in origin. However, only in WSl were the palatalized sibilants identified with the pre-existing alveopalatal fricatives, which conditioned the progressive assimilation of the stops.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_84.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-084.png)

In Novg, where the palatalization halted at the 2VP¹ stage, the reflex is *skʲ, as expected: <i>proskipomъ</i> ‘pierced through (<span class="small-caps">PRPP</span>.<span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">INDEF</span>)’. Less expectedly, the same reflex is well attested in OESl texts of non-Novg origin: <i>rusьskěi</i> ‘Rus’ (<span class="small-caps">F</span>.<span class="small-caps">LOC</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>.<span class="small-caps">DEF</span>)’; *<i>skai̯p</i>- > <i>skěpanije</i> ‘splintering’<i>, oskěpъ</i> ‘spear’<i>, (o)skěpišče</i> ‘spear shaft’<i>, poskěpaša</i> ‘hack up (<span class="small-caps">AOR</span>.3<span class="small-caps">PL</span>)’; *<i>skai̯mεi̯m</i>- <i>> skěmim-</i> ‘press (<span class="small-caps">PRPP</span>)’, cf. (7). The same outcome is attested in the modern languages: Uk <i>skipka</i> ‘chip’, BR <i>skepka</i> ‘pinch’, SRu <i>raskep</i> ‘split’. However, in OESl texts, *skʲ is more often reflected as <i>sts</i> or <i>st</i>, and *zgʲ is meagerly reflected as <i>zd<b>:</b> rusьscěi~rusьstěi; drʲazdě</i> (9). These OCS-like reflexes appear only in alternating (stem-final) position (6, 9); in root-initial position, <i>st</i> is unattested, and <i>sts</i> only crops up in the rare word <i>scěglъ</i> (7)<i></i>, an element of the ChSl register borrowed from OCS. This points to the conclusion that the alternation <i>sk</i>|<i>st</i> arose in imitation of OCS protographs; this was probably true for <i>sts</i>, though that could also develop by analogy to the alternation of non-cluster <i>k</i>|<i>ts</i>.

There are also lexemes in which 2VP *skʲ seems to be reflected as quasi-WSl <i>ʃtʃ</i>: Uk <i>ščemyty</i>, BR <i>ščemic’</i>, Ru <i>ščemit’</i> ‘press’; Uk <i>ščepyty</i>, BR <i>ščepac’</i>, Ru <i>ščepit’</i> ‘splinter’. However, these are probably <i>e-</i> or zero-grades with the regular 1VP reflex of *skʲ; cf. Ru <i>ščomy</i> ‘pincers’ (*<i>skim</i>- or *<i>skem</i>-), OPo <i>szczmić</i> ‘press’ (*<i>skim</i>-); OESl <i>ščьpь</i> ‘waning crescent moon’, <i>ščopy</i> ‘splinters’, OCz <i>ščpieti</i> ‘produce a stinging odor’ (*<i>skip</i>-); Uk <i>ščyryty</i> ‘bare one’s teeth’ (*<i>skεi̯r</i>-), contrast <i>škiryty</i> (8), reflecting o-grade of the same root. These lexemes aside, the evidence points to *skʲ as the pan-ESl − not just Novg − reflex of *skʲ. In ESl, as in WSl, the preceding sibilant evidently arrested the coronalization process.

In relative chronology, the <i>terminus a quo</i> for 2VP¹ is generally considered to be the monophthongization of *ai̯ (4.1). In a late-6<sup>th</sup>-c. Byzantine text, Κελαγάστου (<span class="small-caps">GEN</span>), the name of an Antae emissary, probably renders pre-2VP² *<i>kɛ:₂lagast</i>-; cf. OCS <i>cěl-</i>‘healthy’ and <i>gostь</i> ‘guest’. For E-SSl, the phonological constraints introduced by the change were still active in the 860s, when the protographs of the OCS manuscripts were composed − hence the perceived need for a special Glagolitic letter «`» (misleadingly transliterated <i>ћ</i>) to render foreign «g» before a front vowel; the scribes sometimes replaced it with «g» (<i>g̑eorgii</i> ‘George’), or hypercorrected «g» to «ћ» (<i>golъgota</i> ~ <i>ћolъћota</i>|<i>ћelъћota</i> ‘Golgotha’).

The 2VP created several stem alternations: in the <i>ā</i>-stem <span class="small-caps">DAT</span>/<span class="small-caps">LOC</span> (2) and <span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">DU</span> (2); in the <i>o</i>-stem <span class="small-caps">LOC</span>, <span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span> (4), and <span class="small-caps">LOC</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>, and <span class="small-caps">N</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>. <span class="small-caps">DU</span>; and in the <span class="small-caps">IMP</span> of velar-stem verbs (3). These alternations have been leveled out over time in some of the Sl languages.

### 4.5. Ku̯ clusters

The 2VP (4.4) was, for the most part, blocked if there were consonants between the velar and the potential trigger (1). However, in some dialects, palatalization seems to have proceeded without hindrance in <i>Ku̯</i> clusters. This took place in SSl and non-peripheral ESl dialects, but not in WSl or Novg; thus the isogloss essentially matches that of the 2VP. The change was conditioned not only by *ɛ:₂ (2) but also by inherited *i (3) and *i:₂ from *εi̯ (4) <i>−</i> front vowels that would have triggered the 1VP in immediately preceding velars. However, if the target velar was preceded by *<i>s</i>, the palatalization was blocked in all dialects (5).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_85.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-085.png)

<i>Prima facie</i>, it is odd that the lip rounding and low tonality of *u̯ did not block the 2VP. Rather than assuming that *u̯ was somehow “transparent” for the 2VP when *n, *r, *l were not, it can be posited that *u̯ first underwent assimilatory fronting between velars and front vowels, at least in non-peripheral zones: *u̯ >*ɥ /K__V<sub>[+front]</sub>. Thus, in (2), PSl *<i>ku̯ai̯t</i>- > *<i>ku̯ɛ:₂t</i>- > *<i>kɥɛ:₂t</i>- > *<i>kʲɥɛ:t</i>- > *<i>c̟ɥɛ:t</i>- *<i>tsɥɛ:t</i>-. The posited labiopalatal glide − a more natural conduit for the 2VP than *u̯ − would not be isolated in place or manner of articulation; syllabic [u̟(:)] (IPA [y(:)]), reflecting PSl *i̯u(:) (3.3) existed at the time of the Progressive Palatalization (4.6). (For other changes with progressive-regressive conditioning, see 3.6.1 and 4.6)

### 4.6. Progressive Palatalization

The <i>Progressive Velar Palatalization</i> (PVP) or <i>Palatalization of Baudouin de Courtenay</i> was triggered by the reflexes of PSl syllabic *i(:)₁ (1) and *in (2). The attested reflexes are the same as those of the 2VP: *k > <i>c</i>, *g > <i>dz</i>, and *x > WSl <i>ʃ</i>, SSl and ESl <i>s⁽ʲ⁾</i>. The affricate <i>dz</i> is attested in E-SSl, Slk, and Lech; elsewhere it lenited to <i>z</i> prior to the historical period.

The PVP had two phases. In PVP¹, the articulation changed from velar to fronted dorsal: *k > *kʲ > *c̟; *g > *gʲ > *ɟ; and *x > *xʲ > *ç<i></i>. In PVP², the reflexes coronalized and assibilated: *c̟ > *tʲ > *tsʲ; *ɟ > *dʲ > *dzʲ; *ç > *ʃ in WSl, *sʲ in SSl and ESl. The voiced affricate *dzʲ was preserved on the NW and SE peripheries (Lech and parts of E-SSl) but lenited elsewhere. It is sometimes posited that the PVP and 2VP were a single change (see 4.6.2); a less controversial position is that PVP² and 2VP² were a single change.

The PVP was more precisely progressive-regressive: velars were regularly affected only before *a(:). By contrast, the 1VP reflexes (see 3.2) appear before PSl *ɛ(:), *i(:), and *i̯ (3), and no palatalization at all occurs before PSl *u(:) (4). The blocking effect of *u(:) is evident where the PSl velar was always followed by the same vowel, as in (4). When the PSl velar was stem-final, before alternating desinences, there was allomorphy between velars and PVP reflexes. This was leveled out − not always in favor of the PVP outcome; for <i>*stiga</i>: (1), cf. OSlk <i>Prěsteg</i> (toponym); Ru <sup>†</sup><i>stega</i>; Ru <i>zgi</i> in <i>ni zgi ni vidno</i> ‘pitch-black’ (literally, ‘the path could not be seen’).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_86.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-086.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_87.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-087.png)

Two relative chronologies should be noted. First, the reflexes of *u̟(:), from PSl *u(:) after palatals (3.3), were distinct from inherited *i(:) at the time of PVP¹ (5); presumably, they were still labialized (IPA [y(:)]) or not fully fronted (IPA [ʉ(:)]). As the PVP was blocked before rounded *u(:), it makes sense that it should have been impeded after a rounded vowel.

Second, PVP¹ took place before MCSl². Unlike *ĩ (from PSl *in), *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] (from PSl *εn) did not condition the change, so the two nasal vowels were still distinct (6) (see 4.2, 4.4). Likewise, the reflex of *εi̯, which was not a trigger (7), was still distinct from inherited *i:₁, which was. (Apparent exceptions, e. g. OCS <i>pomidzati</i>, have morphological explanations; see 4.6.1). Scholars who treat the PVP¹ as an ECSl phenomenon (see 4.6.2) argue that the nasal in PSl *in had become *ɲ between *i(:) and velars; this *ɲ was transparent or a trigger for PVP¹, unlike the dental nasal in *ɛn. This ad-hoc explanation fails to explain why other consonants (*l, *r *s, and *z) did not retract (e.g., *l > *ʎ) in the same environment and remained “opaque” for PVP¹. In any case, it is hard to swallow the notion that *ɲ would be a better vector for PVP¹ than *i̯ (7−8).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_88.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-088.png)

Borrowings to and from Sl indicate that PVP¹ was operating in the 6<sup>th</sup>−7<sup>th</sup> centuries. Thus *<i>pɛ:ning</i>- (2) came from a source with WGmc umlaut (*<i>panning</i>- > *<i>penning</i>-; from the late 5<sup>th</sup> c.); the term is attested in WGmc from the mid-7<sup>th</sup> c. (There is no known cognate in EGmc; Go had <i>skatts</i> in the given meaning.) This <i>terminus a quo</i> corresponds to the period of Sl settlement in ECen Europe and hence more extensive contacts with WGmc. (For ‘shilling’, also borrowed from Gmc, Go could be the source: OCS <i>skъlędzъ</i>, OESl <i>stьlʲadzь</i>, Cr †<i>clez</i>.) Likewise, P-Sln borrowed the La hydronym <i>Longati<b>c</b>um</i> before the completion of PVP¹: *<i>lãgati<b>k</b></i>- > <i>Logate<b>c</b></i>. P-Sln settlements in the Eastern Alps date to the late 6<sup>th</sup>−7<sup>th</sup> centuries.

P-ESSl toponyms in southern and central Greece have also been cited for absolute chronology: *<i>au̯ari<b>k</b></i>- ‘sycamore (<span class="small-caps">DIM</span>)’ ⥬ Ἀβαρῖκος; Γαρδίκι (attested in multiple locales) from *<i>gardika2s</i> ‘walled town’; etc. (Vasmer 1941: 301). These loanwords, probably accessed in the 6<sup>th</sup>−8<sup>th</sup> centuries, do not necessarily show the absence of PVP¹, given the limitations of the Gk writing system and the possibility of leveling within Sl. The soft declension of Γαρδίκι may perhaps be due to non-Slavophone perceptions of CSl *kʲ or *c̟ as *ki<i></i>. However, the loanwords may suggest that their Sl models had not completed PVP² at the time of accession; cf. the cognate toponyms Ἀβαρινίτσα<i></i>, Γαρδίτσα<i></i>, which reflect PVP². Vasmer (1941: 301−302) cautions that the Sl suffixes may have been contaminated with the Gk suffix <i>-ikeia</i>.

It is debatable whether PVP² tapered off on the northeastern periphery, as 2VP had (see 4.4−4.5). In Novg, *k after PVP triggers is mostly reflected as <i>ts: otьcь</i> ‘father (<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>)’, <i>věvericě</i> ‘currency (<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>)’ (*<i>u̯ai̯u̯eri:k</i>-); exceptions may be analogical in origin. For * g in the PVP environment<i></i>, Novg has <i>k(ъ)nʲaz-</i> ‘prince’ but <i>leg (</i>÷<i>lьg-)</i> ‘be permitted’ (*<i>lig</i>-). The former could be a borrowing from Kievan; the latter could be due to stem-leveling. For *x, there is a near-complete paradigm of *<i>u̯ix</i>- ‘all’ with stem-final <i>x</i> and hard type endings: <i>voxo (</i>÷<i>vъxo), vъxoě</i>, etc., corresponding to Kievan OESl <i>vьsь</i> (<span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>), <i>vьse</i> (<span class="small-caps">N</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>), <i>vьseě</i> (<span class="small-caps">F</span>.<span class="small-caps">GEN</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>). On this basis, Zaliznjak (2004: 45−46) concludes that P-Novg *x did not undergo the PVP. However, most of the relevant forms do not represent the PVP¹ triggering environment, since they show the change of *<i>u̯ix</i>- > *<i>u̯ux</i>- (see Zaliznjak 2004: 54−55) − hence the spellings <i>vъx-</i> or <i>vox-</i> instead of <i>vьx-</i> or <i>vex-</i>. Zaliznjak suggests (2004) that the vowel backing did not occur when there was a front vowel in the following syllable, based on the form <i>vьxemo</i> (÷<i>vьxěmъ</i> <span class="small-caps">DAT</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>; OESl <i>vьsěmъ</i>). However, this may have been a contamination from ChSl or a mistake; the token dates from the time when “weak” <i>ъ</i> and <i>ь</i> were being lost. Cf. <i>voxь</i> (÷<i>vъxe</i>)<i></i>, with the early Novg hard-stem(!) <span class="small-caps">M</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span> ending <i>-e</i>. Until the chronology of *u̯ix > *u̯ux- is clarified, the root ‘all’ cannot be considered secure evidence that P-Novg *x was unaffected by the PVP.

#### 4.6.1. Leveling of stem alternants

Several suffixes are attested both with and without PVP outcomes. While some of the variation may have arisen in the historical period, others are pan-Slavic. For example, in the suffix *<i>-(in)i:k</i>-, the generalization of the unpalatalized consonant to signal masculine and the palatalized one to signal feminine (or common gender) was undoubtedly prehistoric, as in the forms for ‘sinner’ in (1).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_89.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-089.png)

The expected outcomes have often been obscured by analogical extensions of stem allomorphs (PVP or non-PVP). For example, the alternations <i>k</i>|<i>ts</i> and <i>g</i>|<i>dz</i> were regular in some Leskien II−III aspectual pairs, as seen in (2). In some LCSl dialects, the alternations were extended to Leskien II−III pairs where the PVP had not been phonologically conditioned, e.g. when a consonant intervened between the trigger and the velar (3); cf. also OCS <i>blьsnǫti</i> | <i>bliskati</i> (regular)/<i>bliscati</i> (analogical) ‘shine’ (<span class="small-caps">PFV</span>|<span class="small-caps">IPFV</span>)’ (PSl *<i>bli[:]sk</i>-)<i></i>. Alternatively, the regularly palatalized imperfective stem could be leveled out on the model of the non-palatalized perfective, as seen in OESl <i>klikati</i> (2)<i></i>.

One factor that promoted the leveling out of the PVP alternations was their semiotic vacuity in noun stems. The 1VP and 2VP alternations indexed specific endings; the basic allomorphs ended in velars. By contrast, the PVP alternations had no indexical value within paradigms: the distribution of *g and *dz was morphologically arbitary in *<i>stidza</i>:|*<i>stigu</i>:| *<i>stidza:mu</i> (<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>|<span class="small-caps">GEN</span>, <span class="small-caps">NOM</span>/<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>|<span class="small-caps">DAT</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>), etc. as compared with unpalatalized *<i>darga</i>:| *<i>dargu</i>:|*<i>darga:mu</i> ‘road’. After *ɛi̯ became *i:₂, the alternations were no longer predictable. If the basic stem ended in a PVP reflex, the direction of the alternations (high tonality → low tonality) was the reverse of the pattern seen in the 1VP and 2VP reflexes.

#### 4.6.2. Chronological controversy

The PVP is the most debated of the CSl changes. The main point of contention has been its chronology relative to the regressive palatalizations. There are four main schools of thought. In the earliest view (PVP 3 2VP), the PVP is a “special case” of the 2VP (see 4.4); the identity of reflexes points to a single event.

In a second approach (PVP < 2VP), the PVP was the “Third Palatalization.” It began while the 2VP was in progress and continued for some some time after the 2VP ended. The rationale is that the PVP has more exceptions than the 2VP; this is taken to indicate that it happened when the CSl dialect continuum was less cohesive. However, this argument is not cogent; unlike the 2VP, the PVP only occurred where leveling could operate, and there are good reasons why the alternations it produced should be unstable (see above). In fact, there is no convincing evidence that the 2VP preceded the PVP. Whether the 2VP and the PVP should be treated as a single regressive-progressive change or as separate events is a moot point.

In a third view (PVP¹ > 2VP), PVP¹ took place before the monophthongization that triggered the 2VP (4.1). The rationale for this claim is the fact that PSl *-ai̯- was reflected as OCS <i>-i-</i> rather than <i>-ě-</i> in noun endings after PVP reflexes: <i>otьci</i> instead of *<i>otьcě</i> (<span class="small-caps">LOC</span>). This can be interpreted as a regular development, with vowel fronting conditioned by the PVP¹ reflex: *<i>atikai̯</i> > *<i>atikʲa̟i̯</i> > *<i>atic̟εi̯</i> > <i>otьci</i>. However, another pattern has <i>-ě-</i> for PSl *-ai̯- after the PVP reflexes (see below), and there are other facts that are hard to reconcile with the “PVP > 2VP” approach. The crucial <i>-i-</i> in noun endings has a simple morphological explanation: when the basic stem palatalized, the declension pattern appropriate for palatal stems was adopted wholesale, except for the marginal vocative <i>(otьče)</i>.

A fourth view (PVP¹ > 1VP) treats PVP¹ as an ECSl phenomenon. Though initially proposed in a functionalist framework (Martinet 1955: 366−367), this approach became widespread under the influence of generative grammar, which ordered mutations from the PVP before those from the 1VP and 2VP. The argument goes that, as an intersyllabic change, PVP¹ would have contradicted the later CSl trend toward syllabic synharmony (see 3.1); thus it must have occurred before the 1VP, like the other prominent progressive change, RUKI. The reflexes of PVP¹ and the 1VP, it is argued, merged before front vowels; elsewhere, they remained stable until they merged with the 2VP reflexes. As PVP¹ and vowel fronting (3.3) preceded monophthongization (4.1), the declension of nouns like <i>otьcь</i>|<i>otьci</i>|<i>otьče</i> (<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>|<span class="small-caps">LOC</span>| <span class="small-caps">VOC</span>) is phonologically expected. Vocatives like <i>otьče</i> and <i>kъnęže</i> ‘prince’ are problematic for this approach; the original masculine <i>i̯o-</i>stems − i.e., those whose stem-final consonants were affected by the 1VP or dental palatalization − adopted the <i>u-</i>stem <span class="small-caps">VOC</span> in CSl, e.g. OCS <i>vraču</i> ‘physician’ (*<i>u̯arki̯</i>-).

<!-- source-file: content/07_chapter01_4.xhtml -->

The PVP¹ > 1VP viewpoint requires scrutiny because of its drastic ramifications for reconstruction. The crux is the relative chronology of monophthongization. There is consensus that the 1VP happened before *ai̯ >*ɛ:₂, since the latter triggered the 2VP (4.4). Significantly, neither *ai̯ (*ɛ:₂) nor *εi̯ (*e:₂, MCSl² *i:₂) conditioned the PVP (1), even though palatal glides are typologically common triggers for velar palatalization. If *i̯ had the same tongue configuration as *i, its nonsyllabicity should not have blocked the PVP; the velar would have been the onset of a new syllable in any case. This suggests that PVP¹ began after monophthongization, and hence after the 1VP.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_90.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-090.png)

To circumvent this problem, the proponents of PVP > 1VP posit that the CSl diphthongs had non-close slopes − *ae̯ and *ie̯ instead of *ai̯ and *εi̯. This solution is entirely ad hoc. While *i̯ can be reconstructed from other contexts, there is no such evidence for *e̯<i></i>. As an opening diphthong, *ie̯ would be isolated in the CSl vowel system, and its glide would still have to become close at some point to yield the attested outcome *i:<i></i>. Moreover, if *e̯ is supposed to be a mid or near-close vowel, its syllabic counterpart only arose as a <i>consequence</i> of monophthongization.

Further evidence that monophthongization preceded the PVP comes from the different outcomes of the theme vowels in the pronominal-adjectival declension, PSl *-a- (2) and *-ai̯- (3). In ECSl, a “soft” subdeclension emerged due to the fronting of *a to *ɛ and *ai̯ to *εi̯ after palatals<i></i>, as seen in *<i>na:si̯</i>- (3.3). Significantly, in the pronominal adjectives *<i>si:<b>k</b></i>- and *<i>u̯i<b>x</b></i>-, whose stems ended in PVP targets, thematic *a was fronted, but thematic *ai̯ was not. The resulting mixture of “hard” and “soft” endings was phonologically regular and morphologically conservative if *ai̯ monophthongized to *ɛ:₂ before PVP¹ began.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_91.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-091.png)

If PVP¹ had come first, *ai̯ would have been fronted to *εi̯ (LCSl *i:₂); forms like <i>sic<b>ě</b>mь</i> and <i>vьs<b>ě</b>mъ</i> would then be highly irregular. To salvage the PVP > 1VP chronology, proponents have cast doubt on the antiquity of *<i>si:<b>k</b></i>- and *<i>u̯i<b>x</b>-</i>, despite the fact that *<i>u̯i<b>x</b>-</i> has precise cognates in Ba (Lith <i>vìsas</i>, Latv <i>viss</i>, OPr <i>wissa-</i>). In addition, they argue that *<i>si:<b>k</b></i>- and *<i>u̯i<b>x</b></i>- adopted certain oblique “hard” endings after the PVP and fronting: *<i>u̯iç<b>i:</b>mi</i> → *<i>u̯iç<b>ɛ:</b>mi</i>. Such a change would undo the otherwise regular “soft” declension pattern; it would have no motivation, unlike the wholesale adoption of soft-declension endings by nouns. There is no reason to assume that the pronouns would be more prone than nouns to undergo “peculiar innovations along with haphazard rearrangements of old materials,” as Lunt claims (1981: 86). All in all, the PVP > 1VP view requires too much special pleading to be credible.

#### 4.6.3. Vowel Fronting after PVP

Following the PVP reflexes, *a was fronted to *ɛ: PSl *<i>li:cad</i> > PVP¹ *<i>li:kʲa</i> > OCS, OESl, OPo <i>lice</i>, OCz <i>líce</i> (see 4.6). This is sometimes treated as a Second Vowel Fronting, but it probably was a new manifestation of a phonotactic constraint introduced after the “first” Vowel Fronting that followed the 1VP (see 3.3). In a related development, endings in *-ī(-) were substituted for pre-PVP *-u(:): <i>*kuningu</i>: ⇒ *<i>kunĩ:gʲi</i>: > OESl <i>kъnʲazi</i> ‘prince (<span class="small-caps">INST</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>)’. This change must have been morphological rather than phonological in nature, given that the PVP was blocked by close back *u(:) (see 4.6).

### 4.7. Reflexes of *<u>t,</u> *<u>d</u>

As noted in 3.6, in the PSl clusters * ti̯<i></i>, * di̯ the stops developed retracted allophones *t, *d in assimilation to the palatal glides. Subsequently, the glides were reanalyzed as off-glides and, over time, eliminated (see 3.7). The PSl cluster *kt was also reflected as *t before front vowels (see 3.6.1). The precise articulation of *<u>t,</u> *<u>d</u> − e.g. [c̟<i></i>, ɟ] or [tʲ, *dʲ] − was uncertain and may have differed by dialect. Their subsequent changes definitely occurred at different times in different dialects. The eventual reflexes fall into four zones − ESl, WSl, W-SSl, and E-SSl.

By contrast, *sti̯ and *zdi̯ had uniform outcomes − *ʃtʃ and *ʒdʒ<i></i>, merging with the 1VP reflexes of *sk, *zg (see 3.2). Evidently, the sibilants assimilated to *<u>t,</u> *<u>d;</u> they became hushers directly or were identified with the hushers produced by the 1VP and dental palatalization (see 3.6). Then retracted *<u>t,</u> *<u>d</u> developed fricative releases in assimilation to the hushers and were identified with the pre-existing *tʃ and *(d)ʒ. (Later, in E-SSl, CzSlk, and some Ru dialects, the fricative-affricate clusters dissimilated to *ʃt, *ʒd.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_92.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-092.png)

Within ESl, the reflex of *zdi̯ in ‘rain’ (2) varies. The most frequent spelling <i>žd</i>, though OCS-like, may reflect a vernacular pronunciation <i>ʒd</i>, which is widely attested in Ru dialects. The outcome <i>ʒdʒ</i> is also widespread; in OESl texts of WUk origin, it is spelled «žč», with the letter for the voiceless affricate in the absence of one for the voiced. In OESl texts of northwestern, especially Novg, origin, the outcome was <i>ʒgʲ</i> («žg»).

In P-ESl, *<u>t</u> and *<u>d</u> became alveopalatal affricates *tʃ (3) and *ʒ (4) in all environments. Thus they merged completely with the 1VP reflexes of *k, *g (see 3.2). This suggests that the development of fricative release was completed prior to the onset of the 2VP in P-ESl.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_93.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-093.png)

OESl ecclesiastical (ChSl) texts tend to have <i>∫t∫</i> «щ» for *<u>t</u> and either <i>ʒ</i> «ж» or <i>ʒd</i> «жд» for *<u>d.</u> While <i>ʒ</i> was vernacular, the other spellings arose under OCS influence. In 11<sup>th</sup>- c. texts, the voiceless reflex could be spelled «шт», in direct imitation of OCS <i>∫ t</i>. The reflex <i>∫t∫</i> was derived from the *s<u>t</u> reflex by analogy to OCS, where *<u>t</u> and *s<u>t</u> both came out as <i>∫t</i> (see below).

In P-WSl, *<u>t,</u> *<u>d</u> were assibilated and became the dental affricates *ts (5), *dz (6). These outcomes merged with those of *k, *g in the 2VP (see 4.4) and PVP (see 4.6). Evidently, then, the assibilation phases of the three changes overlapped in P-WSl. The fact that CzSlk patterns with Lech rather than W-SSl may indicate that the change was completed before LCSl (see 4.1). (In Sorb, Cz, and Kb, the reflex of *<u>d</u> ultimately lost its closure.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_94.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-094.png)

In W-SSl and western E-SSl dialects (7−8), MCSl *c̟, *ɟ were conserved longer than elsewhere; their eventual resolutions belong to the post-CSl period. Unassibilated palatal stops («k[ь]» and «g[ь]») are found in the oldest Sb texts (12<sup>th</sup>−14<sup>th</sup> centuries). (The clusters <i>št</i> and <i>žd</i>, borrowed from OCS, [see below] were preferred in texts of the ecclesiastical [Sb-ChSl] register.) Unassibilated palatal stops are also preserved in some Što and Čak dialects, in much of Mc, including the standard, and in some WBg dialects. In other Mc and WBg dialects, the stops have advanced to dental <i>tʲ</i> and <i>dʲ</i>, but without assibilation.

In Sln, Čak, and Kaj, *c̟ typically merged with <i>t∫</i> from the 1VP («č»), while *ɟ lenited through *ɣʲ to *i̯ («j»). In the OSln Freising Fragments (later 10<sup>th</sup> c.), which were written through the filter of OHG perceptions, the *c̟ reflex can be spelled as a stop («k») or as an affricate («z, c, c∫, t∫, ∫»). The *ɟ reflex is rendered «g» or «i», which can be interpreted as the intermediate stage *ɣʲ or as the end result *i̯. The outcomes <i>t∫</i> («č») and <i>i̯</i> («j, ћ») are also attested in medieval Cr ChSl, though the quasi-OCS <i>št</i> and <i>žd</i> are more frequent.

In most modern Što dialects, including standard Bo, Cr, and Sb, the primary palatals assibilated. In the standard, and in many dialects, they are now palatal affricates <i>tɕ</i> and <i>dʑ</i> (also in some Kaj dialects). In other dialects, dental <i>tʃ, dʒ</i> are found.

In much of E-SSl, *c̟, *ɟ became the clusters *ʃtʃ, *ʒdʒ, merging with the reflexes of *sti̯, *zdi̯. These reflexes are preserved in some WBg and SMc dialects. In EBg, including Cyrillic OCS and the modern standard, the clusters simplified to <i>št, žd</i>.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_95.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-095.png)

To account for the E-SSl clusters, some scholars have posited gemination followed by lenition (*<u>t</u> > *<u>tt</u> > *ʃt; *<u>d</u> > *dd > *ʒd), parallel to the PreSl degeminations of *tt > *st and *dd > *zd (see 2.7.2). According to Lunt (2001: 188), this occurred “to fit the constraint that the first of two obstruents must be a sibilant.” The problem is how such geminates could have arisen in the first place, given the constraint, which was already in operation in ECSl (see 3.1.2). Velcheva (1988: 74−75) proposes that the degeminations of {*tt, *dd} and {*<u>tt, dd</u>} (delayed-release consonants) were not just parallel but actually simultaneous. This claim is anachronistic; the first change was a PIE dialectal development, while the second was limited to a single sub-sub-branch of LCSl. (Velcheva 1988: 74 treats the geminates as delayed-release stops; this also seems problematic, since the husher precedes rather than follows the stop or affricate portion.)

### 4.8. *tl, *dl clusters

The clusters *tl and *dl, which were permitted in ECSl syllable structure (see 3.1.1), were subject to changes in MCSl. Their reflexes fall into two main zones: the clusters were preserved in WSl, but lost in most of SSl and in non-peripheral ESl: *tl > *dl > <i>l</i>; *dl > <i>l</i>. (Apparent counterexamples with <i>tl</i> and <i>dl</i> arose due to metathesis [5.5] or the jer-shift [5.8]: OCS <i>dlanь</i> < *<i>dalnis</i> ‘palm’; Bg, Ru <i>metla</i> < *<i>mɛtila</i>: ‘broom’.) In transitional CenSlk and NWSln, the stops were preserved across boundaries (2) but underwent elision within morphemes (3); elsewhere in Sln, the clusters are only preserved across boundaries (1 & 2).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_96.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-096.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_97.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-097.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_98.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-098.png)

In regions with Ba substrata, the dental stops became velars before *<i>l</i>: NEPo (Mazowian): <i>moglitwa</i> ‘prayer’; Old Pskovian (NW Ru): <i>sočkle sʲa</i> ‘settle accounts (<span class="small-caps">RES</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>)’ (*<i>su-kitl</i>-), <i>veglě</i> ‘lead (<span class="small-caps">RES</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>)’ (<i>*u̯ɛdl</i>-).

### 4.9. Initial vowel-sonorant diphthongs

Initial tautosyllabic *aR underwent metathesis, with lexicalized exceptions on the peripheries of Lech and E-SSl. (The handful of lexemes in which initial *εR has been reconstructed pose great problems and will not be discussed here.)

The reflexes of the metathesis fall into two zones; the isogloss must have formed before the late 9<sup>th</sup>−early 10<sup>th</sup> c., when the WSl-W-SSl continuum was disrupted by the Magyar invasion of the Carpathian Basin. In the southern zone (SSl and CenSlk), *a was lengthened in all environments, so that the bimoraicity of the old diphthongs was preserved (1−2): *aRC > *Ra:C. In the northern zone (Sorb, CzSlk, non-peripheral Lech, and ESl), the vowel was only lengthened (1) if it bore an acute accent (see 6.3−6.3.4). Elsewhere, it remained short (2): *ȁ RC > *Ra:C, *aRC > *RaC. In all dialects, *Ra:C was reflected as <i>RaC</i>, and *RaC as <i>RoC</i>, once quantitative differences yielded to qualitative ones (see 5.1).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_99.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-099.png)

In OESl of the ecclesiastical register (ChSl), forms with <i>ra-</i> and <i>la-</i> for expected *ro-and *lo- often appear in imitation of OCS protographs; some have become part of the standard Ru lexicon: <i>razum</i> ‘reason’; <b><i>ra</i></b><i>znyj|</i><sup>†</sup><b><i>ro</i></b><i>znyj</i> ‘various, separate’.

Initial metathesis had not yet run its course during the Slavophone migrations into the Balkans (ca. 6<sup>th</sup>−7<sup>th</sup> centuries). The name *<i>ardagasta²s</i> (post-metathesis ‘<i>Radogostъ</i>’) is attested as Ἀρδάγαστας in an early 7<sup>th</sup>-c. Byzantine source. There are also many toponyms in Greece that were borrowed from CSl in an unmetathesized form. In the northwestern Balkans, Slavophones were already settling near Albona in Istria and near the Byzantine fortress of Arsa in present-day South Serbia by the late 6<sup>th</sup> or early 7<sup>th</sup> c. Likewise, metathesis had not happened in the western Balkans in the late 6<sup>th</sup>−early 7<sup>th</sup> centuries. Thus the name of the Istrian city of Albona<i></i>, Rom *<i>albuna</i> was borrowed in time to undergo metathesis: MCSl² *<i>albu:n</i>- > *<i>albɨn</i> > OCr <i>Labin-</i>. The same is true of the name of the Byzantine fortress Arsa in what is now southern Serbia: MCSl² *<i>arsʊ</i> > OSb <i>Rasь</i>. As a <i>terminus ad quem</i>, the change was a <i>fait accompli</i> for the Slavophones near Thessalonica by the 820s−830s, when Methodius and Cyril acquired the dialect.

The sequence *ar- was still possible in P-WSl when Slavophones settled near the Elbe, no later than the 6<sup>th</sup> c.
