They borrowed the name of the river from OHG or Rom; it was later subject to metathesis, like native words: OHG <i>Albiz</i>, La <i>Albis</i> ⥬ MCSl² *<i>a:lb(i̯)a</i>: > LCSl¹ *<i>lab(i̯)a</i> > OCz <i>Labě</i>, US <i>Łobjo</i>, OPo <i>Łaba</i>, Pb <i>÷Lobü</i>. (The <i>o</i> in US and Pb is due to later developments.) The change was definitely finished in CzSlk by the 9<sup>th</sup> c., as shown by proper names in La sources: <i>Rastiz (Rastica)</i> for *<i>arstislāṷas</i> in the Annals of Fulda (later 9c), cf. OCz <i>Rostislav</i>. The earliest WSl texts, the Kiev Folia (early 10<sup>th</sup> c.), show consistent metathesis.

In the northeast, metathesis had not yet been completed in the early 9<sup>th</sup> c., when Slavophone settlers near Novgorod borrowed the BFi toponym *<i>aaldokas</i> ‘wavy’ ⥬ MCSl² *<i>aldaga</i>: > LCSl¹ *<i>ladɔga</i> > OESl <i>Ladoga</i>. The location became important with the founding of the Norse trading post of <i>Aldeigja</i> (<i>Aldeigjuborg</i>) in 753. Conversely, Vepsian borrowed the name of the Slavophone settlement *<i>ȁ rdagasti̭a</i>- prior to metathesis: <i>Arśkaht</i> (Ru <i>Radogošča</i>).

By ca. 900, the metathesis was over: Old Norse <i>Helgi</i> ⥬ P-ESl *<i>ɔlˠɪgʊ</i> (with svarabhakti) > LCSl¹ *<i>ɔlɪgʊ</i> > OESl Olьgъ (ruler of Rus’ from 881−912).

#### 4.9.1. Exceptional outcomes

In OCS (10<sup>th</sup>−11<sup>th</sup> centuries, with protographs from the later 9<sup>th</sup>−10<sup>th</sup> centuries), the change is complete for all lexemes with initial *ar- and for virtually all with initial *al-<i></i>.The exceptions are two roots that show variation between metathesized and unmetathesized forms: *<i>ȁ lk(a:)-</i> ‘hunger’ (6 <i>la-</i> vs. 19 <i>al-</i>, 9 <i>alъ-</i>, 7 <i>al’-</i>); *<i>aldii̯</i>- ‘boat’ (10 <i>la-</i>, 2 <i>al’d-</i>, 1 <i>ald-</i>). These forms were lexicalized from contacts with conservative speakers. They are also attested in MBg, along with <i>al(ъ)nь</i> ‘deer’ (PSl *<i>alnis</i>), and in some modern dialects (Bg <sup>†</sup><i>alne</i> ‘young chamois’). Some of the OCS copyists found the syllable-final *l exotic − hence the epenthetic schwas in <i>alъ-, al’-</i>. Similar svarabhakti can be seen in the loanwords for ‘altar’, borrowed in missionary contacts of ca. the later 7<sup>th</sup>−early 9<sup>th</sup> centuries: OHG <i>altâri</i> ⥬ OCS <i>olъtarʲ-</i>|<i>ol’tarʲ-</i>|<i>oltarʲ-</i>; Gk ἀλτάριον ⥬ OCS <i>al’tarʲ-</i>|<i>altarʲ-</i>. The centrality of the altar to religious ritual would favor preservation or restoration of the conservative form. (The lexicalized <i>alk-</i>, as in ‘hungering after righteous’, spread from OCS to other ChSl recensions.)

## 5. Late Common Slavic (LCSl) changes

As noted in 1.1, it is impossible to fix a precise upper time limit for the LCSl period. Some LCSl changes were completed in some dialects before they had begun in others. Moreover, some LCSl changes overlapped with changes that belong to the histories of the individual languages. What defines a change as “LCSl” is thus not the “real time” in which it was actualized but the fact that its impetus came from the structure of the LCSl linguistic system (see Andersen 1977, 1986). For this reason, it is a moot point whether the LCSl changes were “shared” or “parallel” in the various dialects, which after the migrations (see 1.1.2) were spread out over a vast territory, albeit with contacts in some zones. It is evident that there were still connections of some kind, given the radiation of loanwords from west to east or south to north; however, the nature of the connections is not fully understood.

In the present work, the LCSl period of changes begins and ends with two major restructurings of the vowel system − <i>Qualitative Differentiation</i> (5.1) and the <i>Jer-Shift</i> (5.8). These are also known as the <i>Second</i> and <i>Third Slavic Vowel Shifts</i>, respectively (Andersen 1998).

The reflexes of LCSl changes typically have central vs. peripheral distributions, based on the historically known positions of the CSl dialects after the Migration Period (see Birnbaum 1966). The actual centers and peripheries differed from one change to another. For the most part, CzSlk patterned with the neighboring dialects of W-SSl in the Carpathian Basin rather than with Lech, with which it was bound in the MCSl changes.

### 5.1. Qualitative differentiation in the vowel system

In <i>Qualitative Differentiation</i> (QD), or the <i>Second Slavic Vowel Shift</i> (Andersen 1998), the old distinctions in length gave way to differences in relative peripherality and tenseness. The resulting <i>LCSl¹ vowel system</i> is attested in OCS (10<sup>th</sup>−11<sup>th</sup> centuries). The MCSl² short vowels became lax and non-peripheral (1): *i > <i>ɪ</i>; *ʊ > <i>ʊ</i> or <i>ǝ</i> (see 5.1.1); *ɛ > <i>ɛ</i>; and *a <i>> ɔ</i>. These reflexes are transcribed <i>ь, ъ, e, o</i> in the Slavistic tradition. The MCSl² long vowels became tense and peripheral (2): *i: > <i>i</i>; *ɨ: > <i>ɨ</i>; ɔ: > <i>u</i> (“u₂”); *ɛ: > <i>æ</i> (see further 5.2.1); and *a: > <i>a</i>. These reflexes are transcribed <i>i, y, u, ě, a</i> in the Slavistic tradition. In the nasal subsystem (3), the outcomes were mid vowels *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]: > *<i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]</i> or *<i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde]</i>; and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]: > <i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]</i> or <i>õ</i> (see 5.2.2). These reflexes are transcribed <i>ę, ǫ</i> in the Slavistic tradition.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_100.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-100.png)

While QD is traditionally presented as a set of phonetic changes, it was principally a phonological reanalysis, in which the previously redundant feature [±tense] took precedence over /±length/. The MCSl² vowels had multiple realizations in phonetic space, which clustered around central or prototypical values; for example, *i could be realized <i>inter alia</i> as [ɪ]. What QD altered was not the range of phonetic values but the underlying relations between the long and short phonemes. The post-QD reflexes of MCSl² long vowels could still be long phonetically; when shortened in final position (see 5.3), they maintained their LCSl¹ articulation rather than merging with their former short counterparts: [a:] > [a], not [o], etc. Likewise, new long vowels developed by accentual retractions (see 6.4.3), contractions (see 5.12), and other changes, but their articulation remained the same: *-ɛi̯ɛ > *ɛ:, not *æ.

QD evidently occurred in the 8<sup>th</sup>−early 9<sup>th</sup> centuries. In loanwords accessed prior to the 8<sup>th</sup> c., foreign <i>a</i> ⥬ MCSl² *a > LCSl¹ *ɔ (4); foreign <i>o</i>(:) ⥬ MCSl² *ɔ: > LCSl¹ *u₂ (5); and foreign <i>u</i>: ⥬ MCSl² *u:₁ >LCSl¹ *ɨ (6). In loanwords accessed after QD, the outcomes were LCSl¹ *a, *ɔ, *u₂, respectively (7). Compare the pre-QD borrowing <i>sotona</i> (4) with the post-QD form seen in OPo <i>szatan</i>, from Latin <i>satanas</i>, borrowed during the Christianization of Poland in the 10<sup>th</sup> c.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_101.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-101.png)

Several of these loanwords have clear <i>termini a quo</i>. <i>Sclaveni</i> began raiding northeastern Italy, where Aquileia (4) was the principal city<i></i>, in the final years of the 6<sup>th</sup> c. They first besieged Thessalonica (5) in the 580s, though they presumably knew the second city of the Empire by reputation long before. They first attacked Salona (6), on the Adriatic, in 536, and seized the town of Nona (6) in the early 7<sup>th</sup> c. (The toponyms in [6] reflect the Dalmatian raising of La <i>o</i> to *u in stressed open syllables; cf. <i>locum</i> > Vegliote <i>luk</i>.)

The renditions of Sl names in 6<sup>th</sup>−7<sup>th</sup>-c. Byzantine texts also reflect the pre-QD stage; *a is rendered with «α» [a] rather than the «ο» or «ω» that would have been expected in LCSl¹ (7). (Of course, the use of <i>α</i> need not imply that the Sl vowel was unrounded, just that it was too open or not rounded enough for a 7<sup>th</sup>-c. Greek to perceive it as [o].) The same reflex appears in early BFi borrowings (perhaps 7<sup>th</sup> c.): *<i>akun</i>- ‘window’ ⥬ Finnish, Votic <i>akuna; *kasa:ri̯</i>- ‘chopper’ ⥬ Finnish, Karelian <i>kassara</i>, Vepsian <i>karaŕ</i> ‘billhook’. BFi undoubtedly had the means to distinguish open and mid-vowels at the time.

There is good evidence for placing the <i>terminus ad quem</i> of QD in the early-to-mid-9<sup>th</sup> c.

Sl names inscribed in the Cividale Gospel (late 9<sup>th</sup>−early 10<sup>th</sup> centuries) appear in post-QD form: MCSl² *<i>katsilʊ</i> ⥬ <i>cozil</i> (= <i>Kocьlъ</i>); MCSl² *<i>dabraʒi:zni</i> ⥬ <i>dobrosisne</i> (= <i>Dobrožiznь</i>). The Glagolitic alphabet (ca. 860), based on the dialect spoken around Thessalonica, has separate letters, transliterated <i>a</i> and <i>o</i>, to render the reflexes of MCSl² *a: and *a<i></i>. For W-SSl and CzSlk, there is evidence from the rendition of Sl names in La and OHG texts.

For ESl, the change was a <i>fait accompli</i> by the 940s, given how Constantine VII renders the names of Dnieper rapids (8) with <i>o</i> [ɔ] for LCSl¹ *ɔ (< MCSl² *a) and <i>η</i> [i] for LCSl¹ *ɨ (< MCSl² *u:₁).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_102.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-102.png)

#### 5.1.1. Delabialization of *w

In LCSl¹ (see 5.1), *i and *u (from PSl *i, *u, and *a₂) were realized in most positions as near-close lax *ɪ and *ʊ/ǝ. In Slavistic terminology, *ɪ is called the <i>front jer (ь)</i>, and *ʊ/ǝ the <i>back jer (ъ</i>); these terms are used for convenience in the subsequent discussion. The rounded articulation of *ʊ was preserved in peripheral zones − ESl; southwestern E-SSl (as seen in standard modern Mc); Sorbian; and some CenSlk dialects.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_103.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-103.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_104.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-104.png)

### 5.2. Post-QD changes in vowel quality

#### 5.2.1. Reflexes of LCSl1 *æ

LCSl¹ *æ (Slavistic <i>ě</i> “jat’”)<i></i>, from PSl *ɛ:₁ and MCSl¹ *ɛ:₂, had a (near-)open front articulation. Numerous Gk toponyms and loanwords of Slavic provenience, probably borrowed during the late 6<sup>th</sup>−8<sup>th</sup> centuries, render *æ as (ι)α rather than ε. In the historical period, open reflexes were preserved in two peripheral zones − Lech and E-SSl (also areas in which nasal vowels persisted after CSl [5.2.2]). In Lech, *æ backed (by a diphthongization process) to <i>a</i> before hard dentals (1), but rose to <i>e</i> elsewhere (2). In E-SSl, the reflex <i>æ</i> or <i>a</i> persists in scattered enclaves: EBg, Aegean Mc <i>v’æra</i> ~ <i>v’ara</i> ‘faith’. In most of EBg, *æ bifurcated in assimilation to the following consonant: it backed to <i>a</i> (1), with no raising of the dorsum toward the palate, before non-palatal consonants, but rose to <i>ɛ</i> (2), with raising of the dorsum, in auslaut and before consonants with palatal coarticulation (see 5.2). In auslaut, *æ became <i>e</i>, with raising of the dorsum (2). In WBg, *æ became <i>e</i> in all positions.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_105.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-105.png)

In Mc, the outcome is <i>a</i> after <i>i̯ (j)</i> (3) and <i>e</i> elsewhere (1, 2). The former reflects the Central Balkan merger of LCSl¹ *æ with *a after *i̯ (3−4). OCS Glagolitic, associated with the central Balkans (Ohrid), has a single grapheme «a», transliterated <i>ě</i>, for both *i̯a and *æ after consonants. OCS Cyrillic, associated with the eastern Balkans (Preslav), has separate letters «ѣ» <i>(ě)</i> for <i>æ</i> and <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-106.png" alt="Inline image: 07_chapter01_fig_106.png">(<i>ja</i>) for <i>ja</i>; however, <i>ja</i> is sometimes spelled «ѣ» due to copying from Glagolitic protographs: огн<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-107.png" alt="Inline image: 07_chapter01_fig_107.png">|огнѣ <i>(ognʲa ~ ognʲě)</i> ‘fire (<span class="small-caps">GEN</span>)’.

Elsewhere in Slavic (5), *æ rose to *e. It has been proposed that this was due to a push-chain: *æ rose to remain distinct from *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde], which was losing its nasalization (see 5.2.2). For most dialects in this zone, there actually was a merger − across the board in P-Ek, P-Sln, and P-LS; before soft consonants in P-Cz; and in final position in PUS. Thus the push-chain hypothesis explains nothing. In fact, tense vowels naturally tend to rise along the peripheral track: <i>æ > e > i</i>. The real issue, then, is the chronology of the raising of *æ relative to denasalization. In most of the dialects where there was denasalization (see 5.2.2), *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] went through the change before *æ began to rise or while it was still relatively open − hence the merger. In peripheral areas (NW Sln, Slk, and ESl), *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] denasalized after *æ rose to mid-position, which preempted the merger.

The rise of LCSl¹ *æ halted at the *e stage in non-peripheral Sln and in dialect islands in WŠto, CenSlk, and Cen and NE Ru. Elsewhere, the intermediate reflex *e took three paths − diphthongization to <i>iɛ(:)</i>, laxing to <i>ɛ</i>, or raising to <i>i</i>. The diphthongal outcome occurred in Jek («je» [short]<i></i>, long «ije»), Slk («ie»), Sorb («ě»), NUk, and some Cen and NE Ru dialects. The laxed open mid outcome arose in Ek, including Kaj and much of Što, Mc, WBg, BR, and most of Ru. The raised outcome merged with <i>i(:)</i> (from LCSl¹ *i) in Ik and some NRu dialects, but remained distinct in WUk: <i>i</i> > <i>ɪ</i>, making room for <i>e</i> > <i>i</i>. In Cz, *e(:) had trifurcating outcomes: *e > OCz <i>iɛ</i> («ě») > Cz <i>(i̯)ɛ</i> («e|ě|je»); *e: > OCz <i>ɛ</i>: («é») after <i>l</i>, but OCz <i>ie</i> («ie») > Cz <i>i</i>: («í») elsewhere.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_108.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-108.png)

The open reflex is evidenced in early loanwords of P-ESl origin (ca. 7<sup>th</sup>−9<sup>th</sup> centuries) in BFi, which had the means to distinguish between open and mid front vowels: LCSl¹ *<i>mæra</i> ‘measure’ ⥬ Finnish <i>määrä</i>, Karelian <i>meärä</i>, Olonetsian <i>meärü</i>, Ludic <i>miär</i>, Estonian <i>määr</i>; LCSl¹ *<i>xlævǝ</i> ‘cowshed’ ⥬ E Finnish <i>lääva</i>, Karelian <i>leävä</i>, Vepsian <i>l’äu</i>. In NW Ru dialects, there are isolated lexemes with <i>a</i> for LCSl¹ *æ, which have been interpreted as relics of the open pronunciation: <i>jal</i> ‘eat (<span class="small-caps">RES</span>)’ (PSl <i>*ɛ:dla₂s</i>); <i>k’ap</i> ‘flail’ (PSl *<i>kai̯pa₂s</i>).

By the historical period (beginning in the 11<sup>th</sup> c.), OESl had close mid <i>e</i> for LCSl¹ *æ. In early Novg (11<sup>th</sup>−mid-12<sup>th</sup> centuries), writers often interchanged the letters «ѣ» <i>(ě)</i> [e] and «є» [ɛ], but this was due to orthographic latitude rather than a merger; <i>e</i> remained distinct from <i>ɛ</i> and eventually merged with <i>i</i>.

#### 5.2.2. Reflexes of LCSl1 nasal vowels

By the end of the LCSl period, the distinction between oral and nasal vowels (see 4.2, 5.1) was only preserved in peripheral dialects − Lech, E-SSl, and NSln (now only in the Jaun Valley [Podjuna] dialect). In these dialects, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] had a (near-)open, front articulation (*<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde]), distinguished from LCSl¹ *æ solely by the feature /+nasal/. Elsewhere in CSl, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] denasalized, for the most part merging with existing oral vowels. At the time of their denasalization, they must have been unitary vowels /Ṽ/ → [Ṽ], since there are no lexicalized traces of decomposition /VN/ → [ṼN]. The table gives the reflexes in the various languages in a no-fine-print version. Among the factors that influenced the language-specific outcomes were vowel length and the quality of the preceding or following consonant. (The length in question developed after QD from contraction [5.12], the acute [6.1.1], neoacute retraction [6.4.3], or compensatory lengthening before weak jers [5.8].)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_109.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-109.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_110.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-110.png)

Nasal vowels are robustly attested in the oldest E-SSl texts in OCS; indeed, the main diagnostic for identifying a manuscript as OCS, rather than ChSl, is that the reflexes of *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] are spelled as the “jus” letters «ѧ» <i>(ę)</i> and «ѫ, ѭ» <i>(ǫ, jǫ)</i> with a high degree of accuracy. MBg (12<sup>th</sup>−15<sup>th</sup> centuries) shows inchoate denasalization; the process has run its course in modern Bg and Mc. In peripheral southwestern Mc, there are isolated lexemes that show decomposition: <i>zǝmp, zamp</i> ‘tooth’ (OCS zǫbъ). (In Mc, besides the standard reflex <i>a</i>, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] can be reflected as <i>u</i> [N, cf. BCS], <i>o</i> [SW], or <i>ǝ</i> [SE and Aegean, cf. Bg].)

In prehistoric Lech, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] backed to *ã before hard dentals (2−3), just as *æ backed to *a. In Kb and Slc, the remaining instances of *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] rose and eventually denasalized (4): *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde](:) > *ẽ(:) > *ĩ(:) > *i(:). Short *ã was preserved as <i>ã</i> (2, 6) («ã», earlier «ę»). Long *ã: was raised to <i>õ</i> («ą») (3, 5, 7). (In ‘ten’ [1], the nasal is probably analogical to the ordinal [3].)

In OPo, <i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]</i> and <i>ã</i> merged in the 14<sup>th</sup> c. as low <i>ã</i> («ø<i></i>, φ» in some texts). Long <i>ã</i>: rose and backed to <i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]</i> («ą») (3−5, 7); short <i>ã</i> eventually fronted to <i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]</i> («ę») (1−2, 6). As a rule of thumb, a former front nasal can be detected from softness reflexes in the preceding consonant (see 5.4). In many dialects, including the standard, <i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]</i> and <i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]</i> have decomposed (diphthongized), in a counterparallel to the MCSl monophthongization: the coda is a glide <i>w ˜</i> before fricatives (2) and finally, and a homorganic nasal stop before stops and affricates. There has been denasalization before laterals.

Elsewhere in Slavic, nasal vowels were denasalized early; they became unitary vowels rather than vowel + nasal diphthongs. In WSln and NWSln, the denasalization took place without changing the vowel height; thus *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] merged with LCSl¹ *ɔ and *ɛ. In adjacent Sln, Čak, and some areas of Kaj, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] became close-mid *o and *e; the front vowel merged with the reflex of LCSl¹ *æ, which had risen on a peripheral track. (There are also Kaj dialects in which *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] merged with *ɛ; here the reflexes are often near-open.)

In a vast central territory (Što, CzSlk, Sorb, and ESl), contiguous until ca. 900, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] rose to merge with LCSl¹ *u₂. In the same zone, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] became tense near-open *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde], so that there was the possibility of a merger either with LCSl¹ *æ (see 5.2.1) or with *a. In LS, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] underwent a merger with *æ. In US, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] merged with *æ in final position and with *a elsewhere. (In [3], the <i>o</i> comes from an <i>e > o</i> change.) In Cz, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] merged with *æ before soft consonants (1, 5), but with *a before hard. In Slk, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] denasalized and moved into the space formerly occupied by LCSl¹ *æ. The reflex of *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] is <i>æ(:)</i> in some dialects; in others, <i>æ</i> has merged with <i>ɛ</i>, and <i>æ</i>: with <i>a:</i>, or else <i>æ</i>: became a sequence <i>ia</i> or <i>i̯a:</i>. In the standard, based on CenSlk, <i>æ</i> appears after labials (2), and <i>ɛ</i> elsewhere; the long reflex is <i>ia</i> (3, 5).

In ESl, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] merged with *a, while *æ rose along the peripheral track, perhaps in a push chain. (This reconstruction departs from the traditional Structuralist approach, which rejected the possibility of a LCSl merger of *æ and *a because it would entail inherent softness prior to the jer-shift. Instead, it was posited that *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] became the distinct vowel *ä [5.8].)

There is evidence that denasalization was not completed until the 10<sup>th</sup> c. in the Carpathian Basin. Hungarian settlers in the Pannonian Plain (from 895) borrowed toponyms from Slk with intact nasalization: <i>Molenta</i> (LCSl¹ *<i>mol<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] ta</i>, a name); <i>Dumbo</i> (LCSl¹ *<i>d<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] bǝ</i> ‘oak’). No later than 894, monks in Cividale (northeastern Italy), recorded the visit of the Moravian ruler Svátopluk I as <i>Szuentiepulc</i> (LCSl¹ *<i>su̯<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] těpl̥kǝ</i>). In a donation charter of 892, the name of the Croatian ruler <i>Mutimir</i> (LCSl¹ *<i>m<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] timirǝ</i>) is recorded as <i>Muncimiro</i>. However, the OSln Freising Fragments (later 10<sup>th</sup> c.) contain only three tokens with residual nasality <i>(en, on, un)</i>; *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] is otherwise reflected as <i>e</i> and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] as <i>o</i> or <i>u</i>.

Early BFi borrowings from ESl reflect nasal vowels: Finnish <i>sunta</i> ‘direction’, Old Estonian <i>sundja</i> ‘judge’ from LCSl¹ *<i>s<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] d</i>- ‘judge, court’. Mikkola (1938: 19) suggests that these date from 9<sup>th</sup>−c. contacts, when the ESl settlers were establishing political institutions. By the early 10<sup>th</sup> c., denasalization had evidently taken place in ESl; cf. Constantine VII’s Βερούτσι for LCSl¹ *<i>uɪr<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] tʃi</i> ‘seething’ (the name of a Dnieper rapid). Norse <i>Ingvarr</i>, the name of an early-10<sup>th</sup>-c. prince of Kiev, was adapted as *<i>igorɪ</i>.

### 5.3. New length distinctions

Although QD eliminated length on the phonemic level (see 5.1), it is posited that the peripheral, tense vowels *i, *ɨ, *u, *æ, and *a and the nasal vowels *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde] and *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde] remained phonetically long. Subsequently, in a stage indicated here by <i>LCSl²</i>, they were shortened in certain phonetic positions, which lay the ground for new oppositions with new long vowels that arose by contraction (5.12), compensatory lengthening (5.10), and neoacute retraction (6.10). The following table gives a no-fine-print summary of the long-vowel outcomes in the Sl languages that reflect the LCSl² length distinction.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_111.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-111.png)

Final vowels were shortened in words of two or more syllables − a process completed before contraction (see 5.12). Thus, the endings in (1) reflect shortening, while those in (2) show length from contraction (except for Sln, which only preserves length in stressed syllables). In addition, long vowels were shortened under or after the ictus in words of three or more syllables (6.1.1). In (3), if the stressed vowel had been long in LCSl², it should have yielded **a: in N/Što BCS («ā») and OCz («á»), and a near-open rather than open vowel in OPo and Slc; cf. also the short vowel in Čak BCS <i>lopȁta</i>. In (4), the OCz root has a long vowel in the disyllabic <span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>, but a short in the trisyllabic forms of the paradigm. In OPo and Slc, the length has been eliminated by stem-leveling. (The BCS and Sln outcomes in [4] have the regular reflex of the old acute accent; see 6.1.1) Polysyllabic shortening did not affect syllables immediately before the ictus (5). If the nasal vowel in the first syllable had been shortened, OCz would have **u-, OPo **ę-, and Slc **ɵ; cf. also Čak <i>utrȍba</i>.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_112.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-112.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_113.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-113.png)

### 5.4. Secondary softening

In some LCSl dialects, prior to the loss of weak jers (see 5.8), labial and dental consonants developed secondary softening (palatal coarticulation) before front vowels: {P, T} > {Pj, Tj}/__ V<sub>[+front]</sub>, where V<sub>[+front]</sub> = {*i, *ɪ, *e, *ɛ, *æ, * <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with tilde]/<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde]}. This is a common type of anticipatory assimilation: the blade of the tongue adopted the domed configuration that characterized the following vowel. This secondary softening had different outcomes from the earlier dental palatalization before *i̯ (3.6, 4.7), since the affected consonants remained primarily dentals. (The earlier change of Pi̯ > Pl did not involve labial softening; see 3.7.2) The hard consonants may have been redundantly velarized; this is particularly likely in the development of *l (see 5.5−5.6).

Secondary softening led to an opposition between hard (velarized) and soft (palatalized) consonants in CzSlk, Sorb, Lech, ESl, and ESSl. Subsequent changes in articulation, illustrated (with no fine print) in (1−3), belong to the histories of the individual languages. The archaic system is best preserved in Ru: LCSl¹ <i>*polʊdɪnʊ</i>: *<i>polʊdɪnɪ</i> > <i>*polʊdʲɪnʊ</i>: *<i>polʊdʲɪnʲɪ</i> > OESl <i>polъdʲьnъ</i>: <i>polъdʲьnь</i> > Ru <i>poldën</i> [dʲ]: <i>polden’</i> [dʲ, nʲ] ‘midday (<sup>†</sup><span class="small-caps">GEN</span>.<span class="small-caps">PL</span>: <span class="small-caps">NOM</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’; LCSl¹ *<i>krou̯ʊ</i>: <i>krʊu̯ɪ</i> > OESl <i>krovъ</i>: <i>krъvʲь</i> > Ru krov: <i>krov’</i> [vʲ] ‘shelter’: ‘blood’

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_114.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-114.png)

In (3), the hardening of *rʲ was a central LCSl development; it occurred in BCS, Slk, Uk, and BR. On the northeastern periphery (Ru), *rʲ was preserved. On the northwestern periphery, *rʲ assibilated, probably to *r̝, an alveolar trill with lamino-palatal frication, which was preserved in Cz, extreme SPo, Kb, and Slc; in Sorb and Po, the reflex underwent further developments.

In SSl, softening is reflected in Bg, but not in Sln, BCS, and Mc. The Bg outcomes of LCSl¹ *æ (see 5.2.1) depended on the hardness or softness of the following consonant. Ultimately, in most dialects, soft consonants were eliminated except before back vowels, where they had arisen in post-CSl changes (Scatton 1993: 197).

The dialect geography of softening indicates that the assimilation and its subsequent phonemicization was a LCSl change, which happened well before the loss of weak jers (see 5.7). (It was also undoubtedly a LCSl change in the sense that it stemmed from the structure of the LCSl phonological system; see 5.) Nevertheless, it has traditionally been assumed that inherent softening developed as a consequence of, and thus subsequent to, the loss of weak jers. That sequence of events is categorically impossible: if softness had not already been inherent (phonemic), the soft allophones conditioned by weak *ɪ would have hardened automatically in the absence of their trigger. Nevertheless, it is highly probable that the <i>reduction</i> of *ɪ facilitated the covert rephonologization of [Cʲ] ⇒ /Cʲ/. For learners, softening was ambiguous: they could ascribe it to the following vowel or, in a covert innovation, assess it as a feature inherent to the consonant: /dɪnɪ/ → [dʲɪnʲɪ] ⇒ /dʲɪnʲɪ/ → [dʲɪnʲɪ]. The shorter *ɪ became, the more likely the innovative analysis became for any given learner. Thus the jer-shift did not open the door for learners to analyze softness as an inherent consonantal feature; it shut the door to their interpreting it as a subphonemic, vowel-driven feature.

There was also systemic support for the reanalysis of [Cʲ] ⇒ /Cʲ/ in some LCSl dialects. Soft *sʲ already existed in ESl (as well as SSl) in the reflex of *x from the 2VP and PVP (see 4.4, 4.6). Likewise, soft *zʲ existed in dialects where the reflex of *g from the same changes had lost its closure. These outcomes were distinct from inherited *s and *z: P-ESl *<i>kun<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] zʲa</i>|*<i>kun<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] zʲu</i> ‘king (<span class="small-caps">GEN</span>|<span class="small-caps">DAT</span>)’, *<i>u̯ɪsʲa</i>|*<i>u̯ɪsʲ<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]</i> ‘all (<span class="small-caps">F</span>.<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>|<span class="small-caps">F</span>.<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>)’; cf. *<i>voza</i>|*<i>vozu</i> ‘cart (<span class="small-caps">GEN</span>|<span class="small-caps">DAT</span>)’, *<i>kosa</i>|*<i>kos<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]</i> ‘braid (<span class="small-caps">NOM</span>|<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>)’. Here, instead of softness, the traditional Structuralist approach posits ad-hoc vowels *ü and *ä. It is unclear how the putative *ü could have remained distinct from MCSl² *ü: (>LCSl¹ *i:, see 4.3, 5.1), or the putative *ä from LCSl¹ *æ (see 5.2.1), unless PVP² (4.6) and the consequent vowel fronting were later than QD (5.1). In addition, *ü and *ä entail morphological complications (at least, for a Structuralist approach); for example, the endings in *<i>kun<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] zä</i> |*<i>kun<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-ae-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: æ with tilde]">[image-glyph: æ with tilde] zü</i> would be allomorphs of those in <i>voza</i>|<i>vozu</i>. According to Lunt (1956: 310), the presence and usage of <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-115.png" alt="Inline image: 07_chapter01_fig_115.png"> and «ю» in the Cyrillic alphabet corroborate the existence of /ü/ and /ä/ in OESl. In fact, they stem from an orthographic tradition that began under the influence of Gk perceptions (see Collins 1992).

#### 5.4.1. Fate of the soft sonorants

The reflexes of *<u>n,</u> *<u>l,</u> *<u>r</u> from dental palatalization (3.6) merged entirely with new soft *nʲ, *lʲ, *rʲ in the dialects in which secondary softening developed (5.4). There was no corresponding merger in W-SSl (1−2), where *<u>n</u> and *<u>l</u> are reflected as primary palatals <i>ɲ, ʎ</i>, and *<u>r</u> as the sequence <i>rj</i> (before vowels only). In Mc, *<u>n</u> and *<u>r</u> are reflected as dentals, and *<u>l</u> as <i>lʲ</i> or <i>ʎ</i>, in contrast to the velarized <i>lˠ</i> from *l dental.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_116.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-116.png)

### 5.5. Internal open vowel-liquid diphthongs

Tautosyllabic *ar, *ɛr, *al, *ɛl (traditionally called <i>TORT formulas)</i> underwent changes whose effect was to remove the liquids from the syllable coda. The reflexes fall into three zones − southern, northwestern, and northeastern; there are two conservative peripheries. The change straddled QD (5.1) in SSl and CzSlk but postdated it elsewhere. The dialect geography is LCSl in that it has a post-Migration-Period center/periphery, and CzSlk patterns entirely with SSl rather than with Sorb and Lech (see 4.1).

In the southern zone, SSl and CzSlk, the sequences underwent metathesis; the vowels were lengthened, so that the sequences remained bimoraic: PSl *ɛR > MCSl² *Rɛ: > LCSl¹ *Ræ; PSl *aR > MCSl² *Ra: > LCSl¹ *Ra. The lengthening took place before or at the same time as the metathesis, given that inherited (non-metathesized) *Rɛ-, *Rawere not affected: LCSl¹ *rɛ, *rɔ, cf. OCS <i>drevlʲьnʲь</i> ‘ancient’, <i>plešte</i> ‘shoulder’, <i>plodъ</i> ‘fruit’, <i>krovъ</i> ‘covering’.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_117.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-117.png)

On the southern periphery, the lengthening stage began before the metathesis. This can be seen from isolated lexemes that reflect lengthening only: MBg <i>baltina</i> ‘bog’ (*<i>balt</i>-), <i>zaltarinъ</i> ‘goldsmith’ (*<i>zalt</i>-), <i>maldičie</i> ‘youth’ (*<i>mald</i>-); Bg <sup>†</sup><i>dalta</i> ‘chisel’ (*<i>dalbt</i>-). In the same zone, the isolated lexemes with unmetathesized initial diphthongs (4.9) also reflect lengthening.

In the northwest, in Sorb and non-peripheral Lech, the sequences underwent metathesis but not lengthening: PSl *ar > LCSl¹ *rɔ (5); PSl *ɛr > *rʲɛ (6), PSl *al > LCSl¹ *lɔ (7)<i></i>, and PSl *ɛl > *lʲɛ (8). In the same dialects, the reflexes of initial open-vowel-liquid diphthongs had undergone pre-QD lengthening under the acute accent (4.9) In the internal diphthongs, the loss of bimoraicity suggests that the change followed QD (5.1); that is, it occurred at a time when length was no longer distinctive in these dialects. The vowels were sometimes lengthened in later prosodic changes − compensatory lengthening (5.10), seen in the US, Kb, and Slc forms in (6), or neoacute lengthening (6.4.3) seen in the Kb and Slc forms for (8).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_118.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-118.png)

Throughout Lech, tautosyllabic *ɛr, *ar, and *al are consistently reflected with metathesis. However, in peripheral Lech *ar is often reflected as <i>or ~ ar</i> (9). Cf. also Pb ÷<i>bordåi̯nǝ</i> ‘hachet’, Kb <i>korwińc</i>, Slc <i>kãrwińc</i> ‘cow-patty’; OPo <i>karw</i> ‘bull’. Evidently, there has been dialect contact in both directions: Kb <i>bardówka</i> ~ <i>brodówka</i> ‘wart’; <i>korwa</i>|<i>krowa</i> ‘cow’. For Pb, *<i>or</i> was the norm; forms with *<i>ro</i> were accessed in contacts with Sorb or CenLech.

In the northeastern zone, comprising ESl, there was neither lengthening nor metathesis; instead, a matching vowel was added after the sonorant: *ar > *ɔrɔ (10); *ɛr > *ɛrʲɛ (11); *al > *ɔlˠɔ (12); and *ɛl > *ɛl⁽ˠ⁾, with bifurcating reflexes: *ɛl⁽ˠ⁾ > *ɛl⁽ˠ⁾ɔ after palatals (13), but *ɔl⁽ˠ⁾ɔ elsewhere (14). This change is traditionally called <i>pleophony</i>.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_119.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-119.png)

For the Uk lexeme in (11), the predicted outcome is <i>bereh; berih</i> reflects *<i>ɛ</i>:, usually ascribed to either neoacute lengthening (see 6.4.3) or compensatory lengthening before a weak jer (see 5.10). The OESl ChSl register seen in most OESl writings typically employed the SSl reflexes in imitation of OCS protographs. However, for *ɛr sequences, the compromise spelling <i>re</i> is more common than <i>rě</i>, even in texts that otherwise distinguish <i>e</i> and <i>ě</i>.

There is abundant evidence that the changes in internal open-vowel-liquid diphthongs did not begin until the 7<sup>th</sup> c. or later. Many Slavic proper names are cited in unmetathesized form by Byzantine authors of the 7<sup>th</sup>−8<sup>th</sup> centuries: Β<b>αλ</b>δίμερ (*<i>u̯<b>al</b>dɛi̯mɛ:ras</i>)<i></i>, Δ<b>αρ</b>γαμηρός (*<i>dargami:ras</i>) − after metathesisis, <i>Vladimirъ</i>, and <i>Dragomirъ</i>. During the same period of early contacts, pre-metathesis forms made their way into other languages of southeastern Europe: *<i>balta</i>- ‘swamp’ ⥬ Albanian <i>b<b>al</b>të</i> ‘mud’, Romanian <i>b<b>al</b>tă</i> ‘swamp’; *<i>m<b>έr</b>gi̯a</i>: ‘net’ ⥬ Gk dial μ<b>έρ</b>ζα. There are numerous toponyms of Slavic provenience in central and southern Greece that show no sign of metathesis: *<i>g<b>ar</b>dika-</i>‘walled town’ ⥬ Γ<b>αρ</b>δίκι; *<i>b<b>ɛr</b>g</i>- ‘bank’ ⥬ Β<b>έρ</b>γος; *<i>s<b>ál</b>minεi̯ka</i>- ‘bed of straw’ ⥬ Σ<b>αλ</b>μενῖκον. (There are also toponyms that reflect metathesis: *<i>b<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-epsilon-hypine.png" alt="[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with overmark/acute-style mark]">[image-glyph: Greek epsilon with overmark/acute-style mark] l</i>- + *<i>g<b>ar</b>d-</i> ‘white town’ ⥬ Μπελιγ<b>ρά</b>δια; *<i>g<b>ar</b>dika-</i> ⥬ Γ<b>ρα</b>δίτσα. These are less frequent in the south, where the hellenization of Slavophone inhabitants began ca. 800.)

In the late 6<sup>th</sup>−early 8<sup>th</sup> centuries, CSl borrowed loanwords with tautosyllabic *ar, *ɛr, *al, *ɛl from WGmc and La; these underwent the same changes as the native sequences (15). Sl names in 7<sup>th</sup>−mid-8<sup>th</sup>-c. Frankish sources likewise reflect the pre-metathesis stage: *<i>u̯aldu:ka</i>: ‘ruler’ ⥬ <i>Walducus, *dɛru̯a:n</i>- ⥬ <i>Deruanus</i> (620s−640s); *<i>bɛrʒinik</i>-⥬ <i>Bersnicha</i> (834). It is often claimed that metathesis was still underway during the time of Charlemagne (771−814), on the assumption that his OHG name <i>Karl-</i> supplied LCSl¹ *<i>karl-</i> ‘king’, attested in all Sl languages except Pb (16)<i></i>. Spellings that reflect metathesis appear in Frankish sources from the late 8<sup>th</sup> c. and become the norm by the mid-9<sup>th</sup> c.: LCSl¹ *<i>tɛrbɛli̯</i>- > P-Slk *<i>trɛbɛʎ-</i> ⥬ <i>Trebel</i> (784); LCSl¹ *<i>pɛrdǝslau̯ǝ</i> > *<i>prɛ:dǝslau̯ǝ</i> ⥬ <i>predezlaus</i> (late 8<sup>th</sup>−early 9<sup>th</sup> c.).; LCSl¹ *<i>sɛbædarg</i>- > P-Sln *<i>sɛbedarg</i>-⥬ <i>sebedrago</i> (late 8<sup>th</sup> c.), <i>Zebedrach</i> (864). Metathesis is reflected consistently in the Kiev Folia (10<sup>th</sup> c.; protograph 860s−880s), in the Freising Fragments (later 10<sup>th</sup> c.), and in the canonical OCS manuscripts (10<sup>th</sup>−11<sup>th</sup> c. with later 9<sup>th</sup>-c. protographs).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_120.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-120.png)

In the northeast, pleophony probably occurred in the 8<sup>th</sup>−9<sup>th</sup> centuries. BFi accessed some loanwords from P-ESl prior to the change: *<i>vɛrtɛnad</i> ‘spindle’ ⥬ Votic <i>värttänä</i>, Karelian <i>värt’t’inä</i>, Estonian <i>värten; *talkunad</i> ⥬ Finnish, Karelian <i>talkkuna</i>, Vepsian <i>taukun</i> ‘oat flour’.

### 5.6. Tautosyllabic close vowel-liquid diphthongs

LCSl¹ *ɪ and *ǝ (“jers”) have different outcomes in tautosyllabic *ɪr, *ɪl, *ǝr, *ǝl sequences than in other environments (see 5.8). The reflexes fall into southern, northern, eastern, and northeastern zones. In the southern zone, comprising SSl and CzSlk, the sequences became syllabic sonorants (1−2); that is, the vowels were reinterpreted as opening phases rather than independent nuclei, in a counter-parallel to the PreSl development (see 2.3).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_121.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-121.png)

In OCS, the outcomes are spelled «rъ, rь» and «lъ, lь»<i></i>, where the choice of the jer letter was orthographic rather than etymological<i></i>. (OCS dictionaries add to the phonological illusion by using entry-forms with <i>Rь</i> for *Ri and <i>Rъ</i> for *Ru. In the tables here, the most frequent variant is given.) The placement of the jer letter after the liquid was a convention influenced by the phonological perceptions of Gk speakers. In original *rɪ, *lɪ, *rǝ, *lǝ (see 5.10), the reflexes of *ɪ and *ǝ acted like jers in other positions (see 5.8); they could be strengthened (<i>krъvь > krovь</i>) or condition the strengthing of a preceding jer (<i>vъ krъvi > vo krъvi</i>). This did not happen with the reflexes of *ɪr, *ɪl, *ǝr, *ǝl, because no jers were present.

In later Bg, <i>R̥</i> became <i>ǝR</i> when followed by a single consonant, and <i>Rǝ</i> elsewhere: <i>vŭrba</i> ‘willow’~<i>vrŭbnica</i> ‘Willow [Palm] Sunday’. In monosyllables, the <i>ǝ</i> could develop on either side of the sonorant. The expected outcomes have often been obscured by leveling. Elsewhere in the southern zone, <i>r̥</i> (1) has generally been stable; <i>l̥</i> (2) has survived as such only in CzSlk and some WBg dialects. Slk distinguishes <i>l̥</i> and <i>l̥:</i>, with neoacute lengthening (see 6.4.3). OCz preserves <i>l̥</i> only after labials; elsewhere, the sonorant has diphthongized: *l̥ > <i>lu</i>, *l̥: > <i>lu</i>: (<i>lú</i>; modern <i>lou</i>). Similar reflexes occur in the northern zone (below). In Sln, BCS, Mc, and some WBg dialects, *l̥ was probably velarized [lˠ]; its opening phase was reanalyzed as a back rounded vowel: Sln <i>ɔlˠ</i> > <i>ɔw</i> («ol», tonemic-system «ɔƚ»); BCS *ɔlˠ > <i>u</i>.

In the northern zone (Sorb and Lech), LCSl¹ *ɪr, *ɪl, *ǝr, *ǝl also became syllabic sonorants. Subsequently, they again became diphthongs − either <i>VR</i> or <i>RV</i>; the order and the timbre of the vowel depended on the surrounding consonants, and in particular on whether the following consonant was a hard (plain) dental. To account for this environment, scholars who view consonant softening as a consequence of the jer-shift have to reconstruct four syllabic sonorants, with a distinction in second tonality: *ɪR > *R̥ʲ, *ǝR> *R̥ˠ; then *R̥ʲ > *R̥ˠ/__Tˠ. This is superfluous; palatal coarticulation developed prior to the jer-shift (see 5.2), and it can also be assumed before *ɪr and *ɪl became syllabic sonorants.

Accordingly, northern-zone *r̥ split into new back vowel-hard sonorant sequences after hard consonants (3); and between consonants with palatal (co)articulation and hard dentals, *or (western), *ar (eastern), but higher-tonality *ɛr⁽ʲ⁾ elsewhere (4). For *l̥, the outcomes are more complex but follow the same general pattern. After hard consonants and after consonants with palatal (co)articulation preceding hard dentals (5), *l̥ split into back vowel and hard sonorant portions (not necessarily in that order): US *olˠ; LS *lˠu (after dentals) and *olˠ (elsewhere); Slc and Kb *lˠu; Po olˠ or *ɛlˠ (after labials, with variation perhaps due to dialect contacts), *lˠu (after dentals), and *ɛlˠ (after velars). After consonants with palatal (co)articulation preceding other consonants, *l̥ had the following reflexes: Sorb *ɛlʲ (after labials before consonants other than hard dentals), *olˠ (elsewhere in US; after labials before hard dentals and after palatals in US), and *lˠu (in LS after dentals); Slc and Kb *olˠ; and Po *ɛlˠ (after labials before hard dentals), *il (after labials before other consonants), *lˠu (after dentals), and *olˠ (after palatals) (6). Pb was an outlier, in that *r̥, *l̥ became sequences of back vowel plus sonorant without regard to the neighboring consonants.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_122.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-122.png)

In ESl, coda *l developed velarized articulation [lˠ]; cf. its modern Uk and BR reflex <i>w</i>. Then *ɪ backed to *ʊ before *lˠ (like *ɛ to *ɔ, see 5.5). The backing was blocked when the *ɪ followed palatals, where, since LCSl, phonotactics did not permit *ʊ in that environment. Later, the vowels in tautosyllabic *ɪr, *il, *ʊr, *ʊl sequences developed like ordinary strong jers (9−10); see 5.8. In peripheral Novg, there was a “Second Pleophony” (cf. 5.5): a copy of the vowel before the sonorant developed after it as well: *ɪr > <i>ɪrɪ</i> («ьrь»), *ur <i>> ʊrʊ</i> («ъrъ»), *ʊlˠ > <i>ʊlʊ</i> («ъlъ»), and *ilˠ > <i>ɪlʊ</i> («ьlъ»). The mechanism was probably the same as in the apparent metatheses in CzSlk, Lech, and E-SSl: the sonorants were realized with schwa-like final phases, which could be rephonologized as independent units.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_123.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-123.png)

### 5.7. Tensing of *i and *ǝ before *i̯

After qualitative differentiation (5.1), the “jer” vowels *ɪ and *ǝ were subject to tensing before *i̯ (1−3). As a result, they merged with the reflexes of PSl *i: and *u:₁ in most dialects.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_124.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-124.png)

In the table, Bg and Mc <i>ie</i> is a disyllabic sequence. In OCz, <i>ie</i> was a diphthong, which arose from the contraction of *ii̯ɛ (see 5.12).

Non-tensed reflexes were preserved in three peripheral areas − Pb, western (Ohrid) OCS, and Ru (excluding Novg) (2). (If there had been tensing in Ru and Pb, the expected outcomes would be **-i-, **-y-, and diphthongal **ai̯, **åi̯, or **oi̯, respectively.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_125.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-125.png)

Judging from spelling variations, the tensing was still an active process in OCS (10<sup>th</sup>−11<sup>th</sup> centuries) and Kievan OESl (11<sup>th</sup>−13<sup>th</sup> centuries). In OCS, there was graphic vacillation in the tensing position between «ь» and «и, ı» for *-ɪ- and between «ъ» and «ъı, ъи» for *ǝ, e.g. sandhi въ истинѫ~въı истинѫ ‘in truth (<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>)’, прѣдамь и~прѣдами и ‘hand over (<span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.1<span class="small-caps">SG</span>) him (<span class="small-caps">ACC</span>)’. In manuscripts originating in the Ohrid milieu, strongjer reflexes (see 5.8) could appear instead: прѣдаме и. Similar variation can be seen in OESl manuscripts.

Tensing overlapped with other changes to *ɪ and *ǝ (see 5.8). In “strong-jer” position, the reflex is generally tense (close) rather than “strong” (mid or near-open). In “weakjer” or reduced position, leveling has obscured the development, but generally, the tense reflex occurs in roots (3), the weak in suffixes (4). (For W-SSl and WSl, the change of *ɪi̯V to i̯V has also been treated as a contraction, but it does not follow the usual course of contraction [5.12].) In eastern OESl, where there was no tensing, quasi-tense spellings of weak jers as и|ı and ъı are probably ChSl imitations of OCS protographs.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_126.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-126.png)

### 5.8. The jer-shift

LCSl¹ *ɪ and *ǝ/*ʊ (“front jer” and “back jer”) underwent a complex of changes known as the <i>jer-shift</i> (Isačenko 1970) or the <i>Third Slavic Vowel Shift</i> (Andersen 1998a). The shift had such major consequences for phonology, syllable structure, and morphology that it is generally, and justifiably, taken as the great divide between LCSl and the history of the individual languages (with the provisos discussed in 5). Among the most important consequences were the abolition of the constraint against closed syllables and the rise of new, previously non-canonical clusters; in the history of the individual languages, this led to new voicing assimilation and final devoicing rules. In addition, in the dialects where consonants developed palatal (co)articulation before front vowels (5.2), the loss of *ɪ in the jer-shift removed the possibility of interpreting them as subphonemic (vowel-driven).

The jer-shift (JS) was not a single punctiliar change. In its first phase (JS¹), there was a tendency, traditionally known as Havlík’s Law, for *ɪ and *ǝ/*ʊ to be reduced (1.0 → 0.5 moras) unless there was an *ɪ or *ǝ/*ʊ in the following syllable: *ɪ > *<span class="small-caps">Ῐ</span> and *ǝ > * <img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-schwa-breve.png" alt="[image-glyph: schwa with breve]">[image-glyph: schwa with breve] (breve = IPA “extra short duration”). There was a countervailing tendency to avoid sequences of two ultrashort vowels. By Havlík’s Law, jers were “weak” (subject to reduction) finally (1) and in syllables before non-jer vowels (2); they were “strong” (not subject to reduction) in syllables before weak jers (1, first syllable). In sequences of three jers, the final was weak, the penultimate strong, and the antepenultimate weak (3). (For the further development of strong jers, see 5.8.1)

In the second phase (JS²), weak jers were subject to further reduction: {*<span class="small-caps">Ῐ</span>, *<img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-schwa-breve.png" alt="[image-glyph: schwa with breve]">[image-glyph: schwa with breve]} > Ø. This process has traditionally been called the <i>fall of the weak jers</i>, which has the misleading implication that the process was sudden. The principal mechanism was not a sound change proper but a phonemic reanalysis, which elapsed over multiple generations, with a great deal of social accommodation. At any given time, LCSl¹ /ɪ/ and /ǝ/ (or peripheral /ʊ/) had a range of phonetic realizations, from short in lento speech through ultrashort [<sup>V</sup>] to Ø in allegro speech. Presented with these ambiguities, innovative learners could covertly reanalyze the weak jers as /Ø/. Presumably, for the sake of social continuity, they learned to produce jer-like paragogic vowels as lento or emphatic actualizations of consonants that were final or pre-consonantal in their grammars: /C/ → [C<sup>ǝ</sup>]; cf. American English emphatic <i>sweet</i> [sǝˈwijt], <i>incredible</i> [ɪnkǝˈrɛdɪbl̥]. Undoubtedly, the actualization was gradual and influenced by pragmatic factors such as rate of speech and style. Overall, innovative speakers would favor null realizations as the best match to their grammars. The null realizations would thus increase in frequency, influencing new learners to make the reanalysis as well. Therefore, the traditional label “fall” or “loss” of weak jers is inaccurate except as a metalinguistic description of the end result.

<!-- source-file: content/07_chapter01_5.xhtml -->

The adjacent consonants also played a role in the implementation. In OESl, the loss of weak jers is first registered between voiceless consonants (LCSl¹ *kʊnʲazɪ > <i>kъnʲazь|knʲazь</i> ‘prince’). Another factor was the position of the jer in the word. Final jers were eliminated earlier than non-final; this is very clearly evidenced in Novg, where null spellings of final jers crop up from ca. 1075, while the main implementation took place in the 1120s−1210s.

The data in the table come from the earliest Sl manuscripts − the Kiev Folia (10<sup>th</sup> c.), written in OCS of Moravian (Mor) provenience; the canonical OCS codices of E-SSl provenience (late 10<sup>th</sup>−11<sup>th</sup> c.); the earliest OESl writings (11<sup>th</sup>−early 12<sup>th</sup> c.); andthe Freising Fragments, hand III, written in OSln (later 10<sup>th</sup> c.). They illustrate the spelling of weak jers in final (1) and medial position (2), as well as sequences of three jers (3).

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_127.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-127.png)

At the JS¹ stage, both weak and strong jers are written with jer letters, without omissions or conflations. This is attested in Mor and in OESl¹. (The occasional omissions of jers in 11<sup>th</sup>-c. OESl manuscripts is thought to be a “bookish” imitation of OCS rather than a sound change in progress.) At the JS² stage, strong jers can be replaced by other vowel letters (see 5.8.2); weak jers can be dropped, written with an apostrophe, or confused with the other jer (ъ for ь in the OCS² examples). This is attested in OCS², OESl², and OSln². While the canonical OCS manuscripts were all produced by JS² scribes, the majority of their jer spellings reflect JS¹; this is due to the scribes’ copying from JS¹ protographs, receiving dictation in a JS¹ pronunciation, or following JS¹ orthographic rules. In the table, conservative spellings are cited under OCS¹−², and innovative under OCS². In the OSln¹−² spellings, weak jers («i») are omitted after the stress, but preserved elsewhere; the strong jer («i, e») (1, 3) has not yet been conclusively identified with another vowel. (The scribe was probably a German copying from an older text.)

#### 5.8.1. Rephonologization of the strong jers

As weak jers grew shorter (JS²), strong jers were lengthened in proportion; ultimately, they were rephonologized as mid- or near-open vowels. This process has traditionally, though illogically, been called the <i>vocalization of strong jers</i>. The reflexes fall into a central zone and peripheries.

In the central zone (W-SSl, WSlk and ESlk, Cz, Sorb, and most of Lech), strong *ɪ and *ǝ (from LCSl¹ *ʊ [5.1.1]), merged as *ǝ or a similar mid-central vowel. (In WSl, *ɪ left its traces in the softness of the preceding consonant [see 5.4].) In early OSb and OCr, <i>ǝ</i> was spelled with the front jer letter «ь»; the back jer letter «ъ» went into abeyance. During the Middle Ages, <i>ǝ</i> was rephonologized as <i>a</i> in Što and most of Čak, and as <i>ɛ</i> («e») in Kaj and in the Čak dialects of a few Adriatic islands. In Sln, the merged reflex bifurcated, depending on length: *<i>ǝ</i> > <i>ǝ</i> («e»); *<i>ǝ</i>: > <i>a</i>: in the southwest, adjoining Čak, and close-mid <i>e</i> («ẹ» or «é») to the east, adjoining Kaj. In WSl, apart from Pb (see 5.8.2) and part of CenSlk, the merged reflex of *ɪ and *ǝ was identified with LCSl¹ *ɛ. In Sorb, this could change to <i>o</i> in US and <i>a</i> in LS, depending on the adjacent consonants. In the table, <i>Slk¹</i> is SE CenSlk dialects, which have rounded reflexes of *u; <i>Slk²</i> is CenSlk, with forms from the standard. (Cf. WSlk <i>déždž, pes, den</i>.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_128.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-128.png)

In the other peripheral zones (Bg, Mc, ESl, and CenSlk), strong *ɪ and *ǝ /*ʊ remained distinct, for the most part. Generally, strong *ɪ was reflected as <i>ɛ</i> («e»); in BR and Ru, it bifurcated into <i>ɔ</i> before hard consonants and <i>e</i> before soft. Strong *ʊ became <i>ɔ</i> («o») in ESl, in part of CenSlk, in western (Ohrid) OCS, and in many Mc dialects, including the standard (though <i>a</i> also occurs as an intrusion from BCS). In Bg, its outcome was delabialized <i>ǝ</i> («ŭ»), merging with the reflex of LCSl¹ *<i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]</i>.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_129.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-129.png)

#### 5.8.2. Pb outcomes

The reflexes of *ɪ and *ǝ did not follow Havlík’s Law in the northwesternmost Slavic language, which therefore was an outlier. Weak jers in initial syllables became strong if they were stressed or in the first pre-stress syllable: LCSl¹ *<i>kʊto</i> > <i>÷kåtü</i> ‘who’ cf. OCS <i>kъto</i>|<i>kto</i>; LCSl¹ *<i>pɪsi</i> > <i>÷pasåi</i>, cf. OCS <i>pьsi|psi</i> ‘dog (nom. pl.)’<i></i>. Other weak jers were lost, as expected. Strong *ɪ and *ǝ merged as <i>ɒ</i> (<i>å</i> in normalized spelling) in some environments, but remained distinct in others − <i>a</i> for front, and close-mid <i>e (ė</i> in normalized spelling) for back: LCSl¹ *<i>dʊzɟɪ</i> > <i>÷dåzd</i> ‘rain’, cf. OCS <i>dъždь</i>; LCSl¹ *<i>pɪsa₂s</i> > <i>÷pjas</i> ‘dog’; LCSl¹ *<i>dɪnɪ</i> > <i>dan</i> ‘day’; LCSl¹ *<i>lokʊtɪ</i> > <i>÷lüt’ėt</i> ‘elbow’.

#### 5.8.3. Irregular outcomes

At the time of the jer-shift, conservative speakers presumably tolerated (or even produced) null implementations of weak jers as allegro forms. Conversely, innovative speakers in the same community must have retained awareness of the existence of vowels in weak-jer position and interpreted them as paragogic vowels − that is, as potential realizations of consonants not immediately followed by vowels in high-style or largo speech. This natural result of generational continuity would have provided many opportunities for irregular outcomes, e.g. insertion of non-etymological vowels in inherited clusters (1).

The regular operation of Havlík's Law created extensive allomorphy (“vowel-zero alternations”). Thus, in the individual languages, the distribution of strong and weak jer reflexes has often been disrupted by stem-leveling to break up complex consonant clusters − though the bar for complexity is set quite high in Sl − or to eliminate multiple vowel-zero alternations in the same stem. Thus, e.g. in ‘stalk’ (2), the ESl and SSl languages have generalized the root allomorph that reflects the strong jer; cf. also modern Cz <i>steblo</i>. In ‘light’ (3), the masculine indefinite form, in accordance with Havlík’s Law, should have a null outcome for the rightmost jer; however, the strong-jer reflex has been extended from forms in which it was regular.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_130.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-130.png)

### 5.9. Tautosyllabic liquid-jer sequences

By Havlík’s Law (5.8), LCSl¹ tautosyllabic *rɪ, *lɪ, *rǝ, *lǝ (peripheral *rʊ, *lʊ) are predicted to yield RV in strong position (1), and R in weak (2). In fact, these outcomes only developed in WSl, excluding Slk, and in the more western dialects of ESl. In the WSl dialects, the new interconsonantal sonorants did not become syllabic; the words in (2) are monosyllabic. (By contrast, tautosyllabic *ɪr, *ɪl, *ǝr, *ǝl had become syllabic; see 5.6). In Sorb, interconsonantal *l ultimately developed into *ɨl (2), with a different vowel from the strong-jer reflex *e. In western ESl, both interconsonantal liquids developed a following epenthetic <i>ɨ</i>, again differing from the strong-jer reflexes (2). In some words, leveling has interfered with the outcomes, as in Uk <i>krovi, sl’oza</i> for expected *<i>kryvi, *slyza</i>.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_131.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-131.png)

The outcomes in Ru differ from those in other ESl languages in that the strong-jer reflexes developed even in weak position. According to Isačenko (1970), Ru actually developed interconsonantal liquids, but they were eliminated, after a period of “trial and error,” by a morphological rule that eliminated vowel-zero alternations adjacent to consonant clusters.

In Slk and SSl, tautosyllabic *rɪ, *lɪ, *rǝ, *lǝ became syllabic sonorants, thus merging with tautosyllabic *ɪr, *ɪl, *ǝr, *ǝl (see 5.6). This happened regardless of whether *ɪ and *ǝ were strong (3) or weak (4) by Havlík’s Law. Exceptions can be found in western (Ohrid) OCS, where the normal strong-jer reflexes are sometimes found. (Some scholars have posited that W-OCS was a peripheral conservation of the same pattern seen in WSl and ESl; consequently, the syllabic reflexes in Slk and in other SSl dialects was a central LCSl innovation.)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_132.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-132.png)

In OCS, the syllabic sonorants of either origin were spelled «rъ, rь» and «lъ, lь»; the jer-letter used was a matter of convention rather than phonology. In EBg dialects, syllabic sonorants were re-diphthongized, with their opening or closing phases reanalyzed as <i>ǝ</i> («ŭr, rŭ» and «ŭl, lŭ»). The tendency was for the opening phase to be reanalyzed before single consonants, and the closing phase before consonant clusters; however, this distribution has been greatly obscured by leveling. (In [3−4], only <i>krŭv</i> shows an unpredicted outcome.) Syllabic sonorants have been preserved in many WBg dialects. In Mc and W-SSl, <i>r̥</i> has been stable; *l̥ eventually re-diphthongized to <i>olˠ</i>, which gave Mc <i>ou̯</i> («ol»), BCS <i>u</i>, and Sln <i>ou̯</i> («ol»<i></i>, tonemic-system «oƚ»).

### 5.10. Compensatory lengthening

Jer-strengthening (5.8.1) was a form of compensatory lengthening (CL): as the weak jer decreased in duration, the strong jer increased. Other vowels also underwent CL before weak jers, at least in the central dialects of LCSl; the process has left no detectable trace in E-SSl, BR, or Ru. In the table, the first form in each set shows the reflex of a vowel lengthened in the CL environment; the second shows the reflex when the following vowel was not a jer. (For the reflexes of lengthened vowels, see 5.3)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_133.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-133.png)

In Sorb, post-16c Po, and Uk, the results of CL can only be seen in the outcomes of LCSl¹ *ɛ (2) and *ɔ (3). In Sorb, the reflexes are either close mid-vowels or diphthongs <i>ie</i> and <i>uo</i> («ě» and «ó»). Similar diphthongs developed in Uk and are preserved in northern dialects. In WUk (including the standard language), <i>uo</i> fronted to <i>iü</i> (OUk «ю»)<i></i>, then lost its labialization; thus it merged with the reflex of <i>ie</i>, and also with the outcome of LCSl¹ *æ (*<i>mæra</i>: > OESl <i>měra</i> > Uk <i>mira</i> ‘measure’). (The reflex of LCSl¹ *i became lax <i>ɪ</i>.)

The regular distribution of CL reflexes has been disturbed by rampant analogy − hence the irregular outcomes (marked <sup>!</sup>). In various LCSl dialects, the implementation of new length depended on the accent type and the following consonant (see Timberlake 1983a). In WSl, another interfering factor was vowel abridgment, which occurred when the consonant between the target vowel and the weak jer was voiceless. This explains outcomes, especially common in peripheral Lech, where penultimate strong jers have null (quasi-weak jer) outcomes: LCSl¹ *<i>ɔu̯ɪsʊ</i> > Kb <i>óws</i> ‘oats’, cf. Po <i>owies</i>; LCSl¹ *<i>nɔgʊtɪ</i> > Kb <i>nokc</i>, cf. Po <i>nogieć</i>.

### 5.11. Lenition of *g

In a central zone of the post-migration CSl, PSl *g was lenited to *ɣ (1). The zone affected stretched from southern WSl (US, CzSlk) to non-peripheral ESl (Rusyn, Uk, BR, SRu); there was a southward extension from Slk to westernmost SSl (NWSln, WSln, and parts of Čak). The lenition also extended into proximal NRu dialects, but its effects can only be seen between vowels: <i>togo</i> > <i>toɣo</i> (⇒ <i>tovo</i>) ‘that (<span class="small-caps">M</span>/<span class="small-caps">N</span>.<span class="small-caps">GEN</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’, but <i>gost’</i> ‘guest’.

Eventually, *ɣ backed to <i>ɦ</i> or <i>ʕ</i> in US, Cz, Slk, NWSln, Rusyn, Uk, and SWBR. This was definitely a secondary development; the reflex in those dialects was evidently still *ɣ when new devoicing rules developed after the jer-shift: /ɦ/ and /ʕ/ devoice to /x/ rather than /h/ or /ħ/ (2). Likewise, /x/ voices to [ɣ] rather than [ɦ] or [ʕ].

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_134.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-134.png)

Given its dialect geography, the lenition of *g was undoubtedly a LCSl change. For absolute chronology, it can be noted that the southward extension must have developed while WSl and W-SSl still formed a continuum, i.e. probably before the early 10<sup>th</sup> c. Nevertheless, some scholars treat it as post-CSl because of names spelled with «g» instead of «h» in La sources and the earliest La-alphabet Sl texts (prior to the 13<sup>th</sup> c.): early OCz <i>bogu</i> ‘God (<span class="small-caps">DAT</span>.<span class="small-caps">SG</span>)’<i></i>. The rationale is that lenition could not be CSl if it happened after the jer-shift (5.8). This is a weak argument. First, «g» is a plausible way of rendering /ɣ/, especially when there is no contrast with /g/, and when «h» was used to render voiceless /x/, along with <i>ch</i>. (In Hebrew-alphabet Knaanic [Judeo-Czech] glosses from the same period, the *g reflex is spelled as velar <i>gimel</i> instead of <i>he</i> or <i>heth</i>.) Second, the argument is predicated on the false view that the end of LCSl was a punctiliar event. Granted that the jer-shift was the last “common” change, it took place over an extended period; its actualization ended in some dialects before beginning in others. The lenition of *g may have had a different chronology relative to the jer-shift in some dialects than others, but it was a development rooted in the laxness of CSl *g, reflected in the lenited reflexes of the velar palatalizations (3.2, 4.4−4.6.; see Andersen 1969, 1977).

As shown by Andersen (1969), the lenition began at a time when the CSl constraint against fricative-fricative clusters was still in force (see 3.1) − that is, before the jer-shift (5.8). This explains why *zg remained <i>zg</i> rather than becoming *<i>zh</i> in ECz, Slk, Uk, and SW BR (3), the dialects most central for − and hence first affected by − the change. (BR <i>zh</i> is pronounced [zg].) As the change radiated outward, it affected dialects where the fricative-fricative constraint had been lifted by the jer-shift; thus the reflex is <i>zɦ</i> in WCz and US dialects (but cf. US <i>mjezga</i>, Cz <i><sup>†</sup>mízga</i>), and <i>zɣ</i> in the SSl and more peripheral ESl dialects in the lenition zone.

### 5.12. Contraction

Following QD (5.1), there was a tendency for Vi̯V sequences, found only over boundaries, to contract to V:. This was one of the changes that created new distinctions in length/tenseness, along with neoacute retraction (6.4.3) and compensatory lengthening (5.10). Contraction occurred throughout Sl, from Pb to Novg; it was most intensive in CzSk and W-SSl, and least in ESl. Nowhere did it reach its ultimate extent; variation between contracted and uncontracted forms still occurred in the historical period, to varying degrees in different grammatical categories. Some of the uncontracted formsmay have been resurrected by stem-leveling, but others were probably original lento forms that persisted as stylistic variants.

In OCS (1−2), four stages of contraction can be observed: 1. pre-contraction; 2. glide loss; 3. assimilation of the second vowel to the first; and 4. monosyllabification (contraction proper). Not every sequence went through all the stages. For example, the assimilation phase could not happen in *-ǝi̯i- or *ɨi̯i, since <i>ǝ</i> and <i>ɨ</i> could not follow other vowels.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_135.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-135.png)

The forms in (2) are ambiguous, like other definite adjective endings formed on the bases *-ǝ- and soft *-ɪ-. As discussed in 3.5, OCS did not have the graphic means to differentiate *i and *i̯i. While the spellings «ъıи», «ии» are clearly disyllabic, they could convey either /ɨi̯i/, /ii̯i/ (OCS¹, with tense-jer reflexes) or /ɨi/, /ii/ (OCS²). Because /ɨ/ was spelled with digraphs («ъı» or «ъи», transliterated <i>y</i>), «ъı, ъи» can be read as disyllabic [ǝi̯i] or monosyllabic [ɨ]. The sequence <i>-ii-</i> («ии») could have arisen by jertensing (5.7) as well as assimilation (OCS³).

The reflexes of contraction were long. If the first vowel was LCSl¹ *ǝ or *ɪ (2), it tensed prior to contraction (see 5.7). When the two vowels were identical, they simply monosyllabified (3). The sequence *ɔjɛ (4) contracted as *ɔ: in SSl, but *ɛ: in WSl (with no secondary softening of the preceding consonant). When the two vowels differed in height, the reflex of contraction matched the more peripheral vowel, regardless of its order in the sequence, according to the following hierachy: <i>close-mid > (near-)open > open-mid</i> (5). The sequence *ii̯V₂ (6), insofar as it contracted at all, became *i̯V₂ or, in WSl, V₂ with preceding secondary softening.

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_136.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-136.png)

![Embedded image: 07_chapter01_fig_137.png](images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-fig-chapter01-137.png)

Uncontracted forms are also attested: BCS <i>sȅ jati</i>, Sln <i>sej̣áti</i> ‘sow’; BCS <i>pȍjās</i>, Sln <i><sup>†</sup>pojȃs</i> ‘belt’; BCS <i><sup>†</sup>stójati</i>, LS <i>stojaś</i>, OPo <i>stojać</i>, Kb <i>stojec</i> ‘stand’. These, plus the lacunae in the table, show the varying extent of implementation or morphological interference in the various dialects.

The contraction *ai̯ɛ > *-a:- in Leskien III verbs like <i>*znati, *znai̯ɛ</i>- ‘know’ opened the door to a major morphological development − the exaptation of the athematic <span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.1<span class="small-caps">SG</span> *<i>mi</i>. The contracted stem in *-a:- was reanalyzed as a theme comparable to the long vowel in athematic *<i>da</i>- ‘give’, *<i>jima</i>- ‘have’; then the thematic <span class="small-caps">PRS</span>.1<span class="small-caps">SG</span> *-<i><img src="images/handbook-comparative-historical-indo-european-linguistics-volume-3-img-reversed-c-tilde.png" alt="[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]">[image-glyph: open o / reversed c with tilde]</i> was replaced by *-<i>mi</i>. The innovation spread to other classes, to differing extents, in SSl and WSl (see Janda 1996). (The endings are given in their LCSl¹ form; the actual spread happened in historical times.)

## 6. Suprasegmental phonology

BaSl accentology has been characterized as the “most complex problem of IE historical grammar” (Watkins 1965: 117). The present account for the most part presents the approach of the Moscow Accentological School (Dybo, Illič-Svityč, and Zaliznjak). For brevity, Ba outcomes are only discussed if shared with Sl. BaSl was a dialect continuum; there is no warrant for assuming that PreSl accentual developments always marched in lockstep with Pre-Ba.

In the tables, <i>AP<sup>I</sup></i> gives the accent paradigm (AP) reconstructed for PIE: <i>1 =</i> barytone (fixed on the stem); <i>1s =</i> fixed on a suffix; <i>2 =</i> oxytone (fixed on the ending). <i>AP<sup>II</sup></i> gives the accent pattern reconstructed for CSl (see 6.7−8): <i>a =</i> fixed on the stem; <i>A =</i> fixed on a suffix; <i>b =</i> fixed on the post-root syllable; <i>B =</i> fixed on the post-suffix syllable; <i>c =</i> mobile stress.
